* LWN Articles
#+OPTIONS: ^:{}

This file contains notes on interesting LWN.net articles.

**Format:**
- [[article_link][article_title]]: article_summary

**Contents:**

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/4974/][Read-copy-update]]: An explanation of RCU (Read-copy-update), a synchronization mechanism optimized for frequently-read, rarely-modified data. RCU allows lock-free reads by having writers make copies, modify them, atomically update pointers, then defer freeing old copies until no references remain. The kernel tracks "quiescent states" (times when CPUs are idle or in user space) to determine when it's safe to reclaim memory. Addresses concerns about the preemptive kernel requiring additional handling via rcu_read_lock() or atomic counters.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/5500/][Thrashing the interrupt code]]: Describes the removal of cli()/sti() and the global_irq_lock from Linux 2.5.28. Ingo Molnar's patch eliminated the global interrupt disabling mechanism that disabled interrupts on all processors, replacing it with per-processor interrupt control. The patch also merged three kernel counters (hard IRQ, soft IRQ/bh, and preemption counters) into a single per-process preempt_count. Drivers that relied on cli()/sti() need to be converted to use proper spin_lock_irq() for protection.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/6403/][The asynchronous I/O core]]: Describes Ben LaHaise's AIO (asynchronous I/O) subsystem patch for Linux. The patch provides the core mechanism for submitting, tracking, and cancelling asynchronous I/O operations via new syscalls io_submit() and io_getevents(). File operations (aio_read, aio_write, aio_fsync) must be implemented in file_operations to support AIO. The article notes that actual execution of AIO operations was still missing - devices and filesystems needed to implement the new operations. The patch included support for mapping completion buffers into user space to avoid syscalls, and handling for cancellation when applications exit.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/7577/][Making Linux safe for pthreads]]: Describes Ingo Molnar and Rusty Russell's work to improve Linux thread support for pthreads. Key contributions: (1) Futex (fast user-space mutex) - allows pthread mutex implementation that only syscalls on contention; (2) TLS (thread-local storage) patch using x86 GDT segments with set_thread_area() syscall; (3) New clone() flags: CLONE_SETTID (writes thread PID back to parent), CLONE_SETTLS (sets up TLS), CLONE_RELEASE_VM (flag for safe thread stack freeing), CLONE_DETACHED (no SIGCHLD on exit). Each CPU got its own GDT, making context switches faster. The goal was efficient thread creation/destruction with minimal syscalls.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/6969/][Large page support in the Linux kernel]]: Discusses Linux kernel support for large pages (up to megabytes) to reduce page table overhead and improve TLB (translation lookaside buffer) efficiency. Linux traditionally uses 4KB/8KB pages; large pages can reduce memory overhead (1024 PTEs for 4MB) and improve performance by extending TLB reach. Rohit Seth's patch introduced get_large_pages()/share_large_pages() syscalls but handled large pages outside normal VM by reserving 25% of memory at boot. Linus noted this was really a "TLB interface" patch. Future wishes included: large page file I/O, mmap flags for large pages, and automatic large page allocation when applications request enough memory. The kernel already uses large pages for mapping the kernel image itself.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/8228/][How random is random enough?]]: Oliver Xymoron posted patches analyzing /dev/random entropy collection, claiming the kernel overestimates entropy in interrupt timing (resolution not as good as assumed, and timing can be manipulated by hostile users), and that entropy transfer between primary/secondary pools was broken. Ted Ts'o responded explaining the design. The fix would reduce available entropy, making /dev/random harder to use. Linus objected, suggesting if /dev/random can't provide useful randomness, it might be better to remove it entirely. The discussion showed tension between "proper" entropy accounting and practical usability.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/8720/][The scheduler and hyperthreading]]: Describes Ingo Molnar's scheduler patch for hyperthreading optimization. Hyperthreading allows a single CPU to behave as two virtual processors; when one stalls, the other runs. However, a naive SMP scheduler doesn't understand the difference between virtual processors on the same physical CPU (which share caches) versus separate physical CPUs. The problem: with 2 physical CPUs × 2 virtual processors each, two processes might both run on the same physical CPU when migrating one to the other physical CPU would perform better. Solution: change run queues from per-processor to per-physical-CPU, allowing virtual processors to share a queue. Smart idle detection (all virtual processors must be idle), plus cheaper task migration between sibling virtual processors. Result: 10%+ performance improvement.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/9620/][Multics security, thirty years later]]: Summary of Karger and Schell's paper comparing Multics security (1974) to modern systems. They found modern "security enhanced" systems lack even basic Multics security techniques. Key quote: SELinux module (1.7MB) is 2.5x larger than Multics' entire 1973 kernel (628KB), questioning whether complexity helps security. They advocate verifiable "security kernels" and note PL/I's explicit string length declarations prevented buffer overflows (unlike C). Prediction: without major improvements, either horrific cyber disasters or effective security technology delivery will occur in 30 years. Also mentions KeyKOS and EROS as successors to Multics security ideas.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/10106/][A new way to sleep?]]: Describes Andrew Morton's new safe sleep API in 2.5.35-mm1. Traditional sleep patterns like "while (condition) interruptible_sleep_on(queue)" have race conditions where wakeups between checking and sleeping can be missed. The new API uses prepare_to_wait() and finish_wait() to safely bracket the sleep, cleaning up the error-prone pattern of manually managing wait queues, task states, and the scheduler. Example: DECLARE_WAKE_QUEUE; prepare_to_wait(&wait_queue, &entry, TASK_INTERRUPTIBLE); if (condition_not_met) schedule(); finish_wait(&wait_queue, &entry);

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/10238/][Solving the process ID allocation problem]]: Describes Ingo Molnar's new PID allocator to fix get_pid()'s quadratic behavior with large numbers of processes (100,000+). Uses bitmap pages with fast bit finding - claims <10us to allocate PID even with 999,999 PIDs allocated. Builds on William Lee Irwin's "idtag" hash table infrastructure for session/process group IDs. Linus preferred a simpler fix: just make max PID very large so the quadratic code path never runs. Ingo argued a large PID space can be exceeded; better to fix the algorithm. Linus put a simpler large-PID-space patch in his tree.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/11351/][The end of task queues]]: Describes Ingo Molnar's workqueue patch replacing task queues in Linux. Old mechanisms: bottom halves (32 max, compile-time numbers, couldn't sleep), then task queues (linked lists of functions, run from scheduler/timer interrupt). Problems: not transparent, still bottom halves, keventd single thread meant one slow task blocked all others. New workqueue mechanism: hidden internals, per-CPU worker threads (so one subsystem's slow task doesn't block others), executes in process context (can sleep), has default workqueue for simple tasks. Task queues removed in 2.5.40, workqueue patch pending merge.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/14168/][sys_epoll - making poll fast]]: Describes Davide Libenzi's epoll syscall for scalable I/O event notification. Unlike select()/poll() which scan all file descriptors each call and don't scale with thousands of fds, epoll uses persistent kernel data structures. Three syscalls: epoll_create(maxfds) creates an epoll fd, epoll_ctl(epfd, op, fd, events) adds/changes/removes fds to watch, epoll_wait(epfd, events, timeout) waits for events. Uses callback-based notification - adds callbacks to struct file, avoiding scanning. Scales in constant time to huge numbers of fds. Initially for pipes/sockets, extensible to other I/O. Similar to NT's IO Completion Ports. Benchmarked to handle 60K sockets on 1GHz PIII.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/14776/][Initramfs arrives]]: Describes the initramfs feature merged in 2.5.46 - a cpio archive attached to the kernel image, unpacked into RAM at boot and used as initial root filesystem. Advantages: smaller kernel code, easier early boot customization without patching kernel, C library and memory protection for initialization code, cleaner interface via syscalls instead of in-kernel "magic". Plan: create klibc (small C library), kinit app, move initrd handling and root mounting to user space. ramfs (not a block device) used instead of traditional ramdisk - simpler and more space-efficient since no duplication between RD block device and dentry/inode caches.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/18411/][How to speed up system calls]]: Describes the vsyscall page optimization merged in 2.5.53 to speed up slow int $0x80 syscalls on Pentium 4. Problem: int $0x80 is very slow on P4; solution: use sysenter instruction instead (much faster on modern CPUs). Solution: kernel maps a read-only "vsyscall" page at top of each process's address space containing optimal syscall code for the current processor. glibc jumps to this page instead of using int $0x80. Challenge: sysenter only supports 5 arguments, so Linus devised a tricky workaround. Result: gettimeofday() runs in half the time on P4, 1.2x faster on P3. Note: sysenter support varies by CPU (Athlon/Duron yes, K6 no).

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/19398/][Two views of freedom and software]]: Covers Richard Stallman'slinux-kernel posts on binary-only kernel modules and software freedom. Two philosophies: Stallman's view that proprietary software always harms freedom by denying rights to study/change/redistribute; versus view that freedom means authors choose licensing and users choose software. Andre Hedrick briefly withdrew from Linux development over this issue. Resulting consensus: binary-only modules acceptable if they stick to exported API; kernel header files (including inline functions) are part of the exported API. This clarified what proprietary module vendors can and cannot do.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/21274/][Anticipatory I/O scheduling]]: Describes Nick Piggin's anticipatory I/O scheduler in 2.5.59-mm5. Problem: disk seeks are slow, prioritizing reads (to keep responsiveness) conflicts with grouping writes (which needs backlog). 2.4 mixed reads with writes, causing slow response during write activity. Even with reads-to-head fix, creates "seek storms" - constant seeking between reads and writes. Solution: after handling a read, wait briefly (millisecond timer) for another "close" request before resuming writes. If close requests keep coming, keep servicing them; otherwise resume normal dispatch. Nearly 6x performance improvement in testing. The anticipatory scheduler can be added to any disk scheduler.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/21379/][Fast reader/writer locks]]: Describes Stephen Hemminger's fast reader/writer locks (frlocks, later renamed seqlock) for solving gettimeofday() performance and denial-of-service issues. Problem: reader/writer spinlocks allowed frequent gettimeofday() calls to block timer tick updates. Solution: frlocks use "pre" and "post" sequence numbers - writers increment pre, modify data, increment post. Readers check that post sequence matches remembered pre sequence (meaning no writer modified data during read). Neither blocks; readers just retry if collision detected. 18% improvement in gettimeofday() speed. Not suitable for lengthy calculations or operations with side effects.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/23037/][Synchronous signal handling]]: Describes Linus's sigfd() syscall prototype for signal handling. sigfd(mask) returns a file descriptor that reports signals matching the mask; reading returns signal structures one at a time. Advantages: orderly signal handling without races, can use select/poll with signals, can pass fd to another process. Linus noted it could extend to timers and async I/O but didn't want to over-extend the interface. Discussion also covered Davide Libenzi's virtual filesystem patch to eliminate duplicated filesystem/inode boilerplate code across kernel subsystems.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/23687/][Full disclosure and the banking industry]]: Commentary on security research by Ross Anderson's team revealing ATM PIN vulnerabilities. Researchers Mike Bond and Piotr Zielinski found a flaw in the decimalization table used in PIN generation that allowed extracting PINs in ~15 guesses instead of ~5000. Citibank sought gag order to suppress disclosure; paper spread widely once revealed. Discusses the broader issue of full disclosure vs. security through obscurity, citing Matt Blaze's master-key lock vulnerability. Emphasizes that banking industry needs external review like software industry. Notes burden of proof differences between US (bank must prove customer fraud) vs UK (customer must prove innocence).

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/26789/][Toward a larger dev_t]]: Describes Andries Brouwer's dev_t expansion patches (to 32 bits: 12 major, 20 minor) and problems encountered when testing with 4000 virtual disks. Issue: request queue structures consume ~150MB for 4000 disks; mempool mechanism used to dynamically allocate request structures. Roman Zippel opposed char device changes, preferring old char device hashing. Discussion on whether dev_t should be 32 or 64 bits. Solution uses mempool for dynamic request structure allocation.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/26608/][The X Window System, past and future]]: History of X11 from 1984 (MIT, X10) to X11 in 1987. Explains why X succeeded despite criticisms: hid hardware details, network transparency, allowed policy experimentation, extensible protocol, worked and was free. Despite being called "vacuumware" and criticized for being bloated, no viable competitor emerged in 15+ years. Fresco project (formerly Berlin) is attempting to replace X with modern approach: policy in window system, resolution-independent API, CORBA transport, heavy threading.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/26899/][The Future of XFree86]]: Covers Keith Packard's ouster from XFree86 Core Team and subsequent controversy. Packard had tried to start a fork without discussing with team. XFree86 board opened discussion, GNOME and KDE issued joint statement. Issues: limited development resources, slow releases, lack of cooperation with other projects. Solutions: David Wexelblat noted loadable drivers mean no release needed for new card support; regular CVS snapshots every two weeks promised. Debate over whether XFree86 should be community-governed vs remain a meritocracy. Concern about lack of commercial support for X development.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/28324/][The ongoing device number debate]]: Continues dev_t expansion discussion. The expansion itself is uncontroversial; the real debate is whether to implement fully dynamic device number space. Roman Zippel advocated for full dynamic approach, but others want to solve immediate problems first (like SCSI disk number shortage). devfs is a solution but disliked by many; hotplug pushes the problem to user space but isn't ready. Decision: 2.6 will likely get expanded dev_t, with fully dynamic approach deferred to 2.7.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/28897/][Managing dynamic device naming]]: Describes Greg Kroah-Hartman's udev project, pushing device naming entirely to user space. Three components: namedev (naming policy), libsysfs (common API for device info from sysfs), udev (responds to hotplug events). Current version 0.1 is minimal proof of concept; issues to resolve: race conditions in event management, multiple programs wanting hotplug events, per-site naming policy chaos. Linux Standard Base designates LANANA devices.txt as authority for standard names. 2.6 will have larger dev_t and udev hooks; full solution likely for 2.7.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/29906/][Novell and Linux]]: 2003 interview with Novell's Kristopher Magnusson after SUSE acquisition. Novell making NetWare 7 run on Linux kernel ("dual-kernel" strategy). Company's open source contributions focus on Apache, MySQL, PHP, OpenLDAP - not kernel. Novell has no definite plans for kernel improvements, focuses on services above kernel. Participates in Apache Software Foundation, contributed Java libraries and DSMLv2 server to OpenLDAP.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/33812/][strlcpy()]]: Discusses OpenBSD's strlcpy() function added to Linux kernel. Problem: classic C string functions strcpy() are unsafe, but strncpy() is poorly suited - wastes time zero-filling destination, and doesn't guarantee NUL-termination if truncated. strlcpy() signature: size_t strlcpy(char *dest, const char *src, size_t size) - copies src to dest, always NUL-terminates, returns source length so caller can detect truncation. Linus merged it into kernel after getting fed up with buffer overflows. Also covers OpenBSD's related strlcat(). Discussion about whether glibc should include it (Ulrich Drepper opposed). Multiple public domain implementations posted for handling overlapping memory regions.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/31875/][What is "unauthorized access"?]]: Analyzes legal definitions of "unauthorized access" in US computer crime law. Problem: laws are vague about what constitutes accessing a computer and what makes it unauthorized. Robert Morris worm case - did he "access" all infected systems or just launch point? Paper by Orin S. Kerr proposes: "access" should be broad (any successful interaction with computer), but "unauthorized" should be narrow (only circumventing code-based access controls). Breaching contract (ToS, spam, deep linking) should be civil matter, not criminal. Anti-circumvention makes sense for access controls as "lock on the door." Concerns: overly broad interpretations could criminalize everyday net behavior.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/30653/][The Linux kernel and digital rights management]]: Discusses Linus's statement that DRM is "perfectly ok with Linux." Hardware can refuse to boot kernels not signed with a particular private key, restricting what runs on the system. GPL argument: private keys aren't covered by GPL source code definition, so distributing signed binaries without keys is allowed. DRM techniques could also enable higher security (signed executables). Linus doesn't want to discriminate against DRM users per free software principles. Ultimately marketplace must decide; GPL can't prevent hardware-locked systems.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/30773/][Suspending the system]]: Describes software suspend patch merged in 2.5.18. Process: send freeze signals to processes (go to STOPPED state), free memory, flush disk writes, suspend devices, write page directory to swap partition, power down. Resume detects image and restores state. Problems: no SMP, no high memory, x86 only, needs adequate swap partition. Issues: swap file with journaling filesystems causes corruption (journal replay conflicts with restored state). Patrick Mochel working on general power management framework that can hide suspend/resume behind function pointers, adapting to hardware capabilities (ACPI vs software suspend).

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/31462/][The right way to yield]]: OpenOffice performance regression from sched_yield() semantics change in 2.5. Old behavior: moved process to end of run queue. New behavior: moves to "expired" queue, cancelling time slice - process must wait for all others to exhaust slices. OpenOffice used busy-wait loops with sched_yield(), causing poor responsiveness with new semantics. Consensus: apps using sched_yield() for busy-wait are "broken" and should be fixed. OpenOffice fix already committed. Discussion: sched_yield() now only useful when truly giving up CPU with no time slice; use conditional variables for waiting.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/21861/][The MS-SQL worm: lessons for free software]]: Analysis of the MS-SQL (Slammer) worm and lessons for free software. Worm infected ~100,000 hosts within minutes, no time for admins to react. Monoculture concerns: eliminating monocultures won't make net safe; any widespread vulnerability in free software could be used similarly. Free software advantages: security updates are small fixes, easy to install; no bundled subsystems users don't know about (SQL Server hidden in other apps); databases like PostgreSQL/MySQL don't expose to net by default. Suggestions: egress filtering, connection rate limiting at border routers. SQL Slammer specifically spread fast due to UDP-based probing (no TCP handshake delay). Rate limiting new IP connections (e.g., 10 new/second) could slow worm spread while not affecting normal use.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/40010/][RFCs - insufficiently free?]]: Discusses Debian bug 92810 about RFC licensing. RFCs are core Internet standards documents, but carry a copyright notice restricting modifications - only allowed for developing Internet standards, not creating derived works. This violates Debian Free Software Guidelines (DFSG). Debian removed RFCs from main distribution to non-free. Internet Society's intent: RFCs should be immutable once issued (changes come via new RFCs with new numbers). Critics argue license prevents forking if ISOC goes bad. Discussion: standards vs documents, trademark vs copyright, whether ISOC's restrictions are reasonable. Solutions proposed: trademark model (can use name if unmodified), or require clear labeling of derived versions. Debian serves as "conscience" for licensing issues even when few users affected.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/39925/][64GB on 32-bit systems]]: Discusses memory limitations on 32-bit x86 systems. Linux historically limited to 1GB kernel address space due to 3GB/1GB virtual address split. High memory support allows >1GB but kernel data structures still limited to low memory. Problem: struct page for each physical page consumes 40 bytes/page; 64GB RAM needs 640MB just for memory map, exceeding 1GB low memory. Ingo Molnar's patch: separate page tables for user/kernel, eliminating split - quadruples low memory to 4GB. Tradeoff: TLB flush required when switching page tables, causing significant slowdown. Not likely in mainline before 2.7; may appear in vendor distributions. Alternative: page clustering, or simply use 64-bit hardware.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/44266/][Too many threads?]]: Discusses proliferation of kernel threads in 2.6. Problem: firmware loading via default workqueue had latency issues; solution created dedicated workqueue but added more kernel threads. Author's dual-Pentium system shows 21 kernel threads (migration, ksoftirqd, events, kblockd, khubd, kirqd, pdflush, kswapd0, aio, scsi_eh, ahc_dv, kseriod, kjournald). Kernel threads consume memory and clutter ps listings. Proposed rework: turn default workqueue into thread pool that creates threads on demand and trims via timeout. Many workqueue users don't need per-CPU threads - could specify single thread. Changes not difficult but questionable if appropriate at this development stage.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/46678/][dev_t expansion status]]: Status update on 64-bit dev_t expansion. Had been stalled waiting for Alexander Viro's patches, now appearing in 2.6.0-test4-mm2. Patches include: cleanup of kdev_t usage (replaced with proper types), JFFS filesystem changes (explicit 16-bit device field for on-media structures), and new iminor()/imajor() helper functions to access minor/major numbers without knowing internal storage. Future patches will change inode structure to use dev_t instead of kdev_t for i_rdev. Important feature for 2.6 to support larger device numbers.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/50703/][dev_t expands at last]]: dev_t expansion merged into 2.6.0-test6. Final format: 32 bits total - 12 for major, 20 for minor (vs earlier proposal of 32/32). Encoding designed to maintain backward compatibility: old 16-bit device numbers remain valid in new format. User-space encoding differs from internal kernel encoding to preserve compatibility. Problem: some ioctl() calls and filesystems pass 16-bit values directly - these return EOVERFLOW error if value doesn't fit. Discussion: EOVERFLOW vs filling in illegal device number (deferring error to actual use) - similar to readdir32 issues. Linus merged the changes; expect some surprises as remaining issues surface.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/53806/][Two views of the Unix philosophy]]: Comparative review of two books on Unix philosophy. "The Art of Unix Programming" by Eric Raymond (available online under Creative Commons) vs "Linux and the Unix Philosophy" by Mike Gancarz (1994 book retrofitted with Linux references). Raymond's book is more comprehensive, covers scripting, networks, modern languages; Gancarz's book shows its age (advice like fitting function params on one line). Key philosophy contrasts from the books: Raymond: write big programs only when nothing else will do, prototype before polish, choose portability over efficiency, design programs to connect with others, store data in flat text files, silence is golden, separate policy from mechanism. Gancarz: small is beautiful, make each program do one thing well, programmer time is expensive, use shell scripts for leverage. Both books neglect security. Recommended: TAOUP over Gancarz's book.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/56739/][Loading NDIS drivers into the kernel]]: Describes ndiswrapper, a GPL'd Linux module that loads Windows NDIS drivers. Works by registering misc device with ioctl() to accept driver from user space, performing relocation, then providing two glue layers: net_device operations for networking subsystem, and emulation of Windows API calls (interrupt allocation, I/O memory, packet feeding). NDIS API is well documented by Microsoft. Criticisms: wrapper approach is suboptimal (no performance/reliability improvements), requires proprietary Windows code in kernel, may discourage vendors from native Linux drivers, only works on x86. Alternative to proprietary Linuxant DriverLoader. Could be useful for reverse-engineering unsupported hardware.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/56923/][The future of the Linux filesystem]]: Discusses Linux filesystem competition with Microsoft's WinFS (SQL-based filesystem in Longhorn). Two Linux approaches: (1) ReiserFS - Hans Reiser's vision of no structure imposed by filesystem, free-form search queries, everything including databases in filesystem. Reiser4 adds transactions, better small file performance, plugin architecture. (2) GNOME Storage - entirely user-space, built on gnome-vfs, uses PostgreSQL underneath, aims to replace traditional filesystem with document store. Neither proposes putting SQL in kernel. By Longhorn's expected release, Linux may have comparable alternatives.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/57800/][On the proper use of vmalloc()]]: Discusses proper vmalloc() usage. Linus stated vmalloc() should have no valid new uses - only legitimate for modules and a few legacy cases (like file descriptor array). Every vmalloc() call requires page table tweaking and TLB flushes, making it slow. vmalloc() space is outside regular kernel range, requires extra TLB slots, and virtual space is limited on many architectures. For large allocations, use seq_file interface instead. Example: /proc/interrupts buffer allocation on large SMP systems failed with kmalloc() - but Linus preferred seq_file solution over vmalloc().

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/64327/][Use of patented code in the kernel]]: Linus clarified kernel policy on patents: don't submit code implementing known patented algorithms, find unencumbered alternatives instead. Known problems should be excluded, but developers shouldn't go looking for patent issues. Proposal to add CONFIG options for patented code rejected - would create partially proprietary kernel. Some patented code already exists: SB-Live mixer code with undisclosed license, FTL patent for memory card driver (only licensed for PCMCIA). Future difficult choices likely as more patent-encumbered code is discovered.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/65178/][Kernel threads made easy]]: Rusty Russell's new kthread API simplifies kernel thread creation for hotplug CPU work. New functions: kthread_create() (creates but doesn't start), kthread_run() (create and start), kthread_stop() (stops thread via signal), kthread_bind() (pin to CPU). Thread function signature: int thread_function(void *data). Thread checks signals and returns when asked to stop. Patches convert existing kernel thread users to new infrastructure.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/65195/][The future of device numbers]]: Greg KH explained udev vs devfs to reduce devfs user complaints. Linus clarified device numbers are "random cookies" not stable identifiers - attempts to make them stable just encourage buggy code. Hotplug devices (USB, iSCSI) already have unstable numbers. udev moves naming policy to user space where it belongs. Proposal: random device numbers during development to flush out assumptions. devfs deprecated but will be supported through 2.6 lifetime.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/66091/][User-space device drivers]]: Gelato project (IA-64) work enabling device drivers in user space. Drivers are 50% of kernel code but cause 85% of bugs. User-space drivers allow stable ABI, any language (even Python), no licensing issues. Required pieces: I/O port access, mmap /dev/mem, PCI config space via ioctl(). Missing: interrupt handling - Gelato patch adds /proc/irq/N/irq interface; reading blocks until interrupt, then returns to user space. DMA: new syscalls usr_pci_open(), usr_pci_map() with USR_ALLOC_CONSISTENT/USR_MAP/USR_UNMAP. User-space block drivers register with kernel via special files. Concerns: some see this as microkernel-like; could legitimize binary-only drivers. Linux.Conf.Au presentation.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/66829/][FUSE - implementing filesystems in user space]]: FUSE (three-part system): kernel module hooks into VFS, special device for user-space comms, protocol translation; userspace library translates requests to function calls (open, read, write, rename, symlink etc); user code fills fuse_operations struct. AVFS uses FUSE for tar/zip filesystems. Could mirror web sites, access object databases, experiment with "everything is a file." FUSE 1.1 release candidate announced. Security: only mount requires root, fusermount allows non-root users. KDE KIO slaves via FUSE gateway exists.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/67194/][User-space filesystems and security]]: Security discussion around FUSE. fusermount handles secure mounting for non-root users. Concern about running filesystems from untrusted sources. Generally safe if only root can mount. See also LUFS, AVFS, userfs projects.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/68104/][NFS in user space?]]: NFS server traditionally ran in user space while client was in kernel. Using user-space filesystems like FUSE enables experimental network filesystems. 9P (Plan 9) client for Linux discussed - would allow implementing filesystems as user-space servers. Projects: v9fs, Ron Minnich's work.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/69419/][snprintf() confusion]]: C99 snprintf() returns length string "would be" if all fit (not actual length written), causing bugs in kernel. Many kernel coders expected the latter. Juergen Quade's patch adds scnprintf() and vscnprintf() that return actual length written. Plan: migrate kernel to new functions. Problem: many places do "len += snprintf(buf+len, buflen-len, ...)" - when buflen-len goes negative (size_t underflow), becomes huge value, inviting buffer overrun. Patch fixes 200+ calls.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/69523/][Trimming down sysfs]]: sysfs uses VFS dentry/inode cache as backing store - one dentry+inode per file/directory. On large systems with hundreds/thousands of sysfs entries, consumes significant low memory. Maneesh Soni's patch: sysfs_dirent structures replace VFS caching for attribute files (70% of entries). Saves 145MB low memory on massive test system; active dentries drop from 60K to 9K. No changes to kobject structure. Getting closer to inclusion.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/70680/][Safe sysfs support]]: Frame buffer sysfs support patch (merged in 2.6.3-rc1) had module unload oops bug. Problem: lifecycle rules - sysfs files can persist after device/module removed. fb_info structures freed twice or static ones freed. Solution: class_simple interface (merged 2.6.2-rc1) handles lifecycle without driver references. Functions: class_simple_create/destroy, class_simple_device_add/remove. Networking subsystem had similar issues - required dynamic net_device allocation.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/71472/][The kernel and character set encodings]]: JFS bug with UTF-8 filenames sparked discussion. Kernel has no character encoding policy - sees bytes, not characters. Only practical encodings: iso-8859-1 and UTF-8 (no NUL or / in encoding). Multiple encodings can represent same filename, causing user confusion. Tridge proposed case-insensitive lookups needed for Samba, but kernel hackers resistant - different languages need different case rules. Linus proposed magic_open() interface for user-space case-insensitive lookups. Unicode normalization (NFC vs NFD) also a concern. Security: overlong UTF-8 encodings can create security holes.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/73847/][A retry-based AIO infrastructure]]: Suparna Bhattacharya's buffered AIO patches use a retry-based approach—when an operation would block, it returns -EIOCBRETRY and is retried later via workqueue. Uses NULL-process wait queue entries to avoid sleeping.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/74149/][Time to thrash the 2.6 VM?]]: Andrea Arcangeli's VM patches address three problems on large memory systems: poor swapout with shared file mappings (solved by rmap/object-based rmap), swapout I/O storms, and preference for swapping dirty shared memory over clean cache.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/75174/][Virtual Memory I: the problem]]: Background on 32-bit memory limitations: 3GB user/1GB kernel address space split, high vs low memory concepts, problems scaling beyond 8-16GB, and tradeoffs of the 4G/4G patch for larger kernel address space.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/75198/][Virtual Memory II: the return of objrmap]]: Andrea Arcangeli's object-based reverse mapping to replace rmap for scalability. Covers file-backed and anonymous memory approaches, introducing anon_vma structures to handle forked processes with multiple page table mappings.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/76153/][The GPL and library code]]: Discusses dual-licensing model (MySQL, Sleepycat, Trolltech) and the GPL incompatibility problem preventing MySQL 4 + PHP combinations. MySQL addressed this with the FOSS License Exception allowing linking with GPL-incompatible free licenses.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/78401/][Merging the virtual memory work]]: Andrea Arcangeli's objrmap + anon-vma + prio_tree VM patches for 2.6.5 scaling to 32GB+ memory; Andrew Morton hesitant to merge mid-stable series; SUSE planning to include in SLES 9.1.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/77972/][COW Links]]: Jörn Engel's copy-on-write link implementation allowing efficient file tree cloning via shared disk blocks until written. Linus prefers keeping inode number unchanged when COW breaks the link.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/77981/][Big block transfers: good or bad?]]: Jeff Garzik's libata patch enabling 32MB LBA48 transfers for SATA; debate over latency vs throughput tradeoff; likely outcome is adaptive dynamic tuning based on request execution time.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/79155/][A new device naming scheme]]: SUSE proposal for /dev/disk/by-uuid, /dev/disk/by-label, etc. to provide persistent device names in hotpluggable environments; criticized as redundant with udev functionality already in /sys.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/79185/][Capabilities in 2.6]]: Discussion of CAP_IPC_LOCK for memory locking and large pages; Andrea Arcangeli and William Lee Irwin patches to disable capability checks; Andrew Morton noted existing capability code was broken and SELinux could solve it but is heavyweight.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/79443/][The first X.Org release]]: X11R6.7 marks X.Org fork from XFree86 after license incompatibility with GPL; transitional release; future plans to split X into independently-releasable components (server, client libs, apps).
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/80911/][Scheduling domains]]: Hierarchical scheduler structure for 2.6 managing hyperthreading, SMP, and NUMA levels via sched_domain and sched_group structures; balances load across domains with policies for cache affinity, migration costs, and CPU power accounting for hyperthreaded processors.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/82495/][The cost of inline functions]]: Removing inline from SKB functions made benchmarks 3% faster; inline replication bloats code size causing more cache misses; smaller executables often run faster due to better cache utilization.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/87814/][x86 NX support]]: Ingo Molnar's patch enabling AMD's NX (no-execute) bit on 32-bit x86 via PAE mode; allows marking stack/heap non-executable; kernel data also marked non-executable; harder to exploit kernel buffer overflows.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/91829/][Reorganizing the address space]]: Ingo Molnar's patch moves mmap region to top of user address space (growing downward toward heap), creating contiguous space for heap/mmap; reduces page table overhead; Fedora already shipped with this change.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/90818/][Separating kernel source and object files]]: SUSE's use of O= build option broke proprietary modules when build symlink pointed to object dir instead of source; Sam Ravnborg's patch makes build link always point to object dir with new source link for external module builds.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/90870/][A handful of DMA topics]]: Three DMA fixes: (1) dma_get_required_mask() helps drivers select optimal DMA address size, (2) fixing page allocator to use bottom of blocks instead of top improves scatterlist merging from 0.1% to 55%, (3) dma_map_coherent() proposed for mapping onboard device memory (remote memory) to user space.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/92786/][A look at PostgreSQL]]: Preview of PostgreSQL 7.5 features: tablespaces for spreading DB across storage devices, nested transactions (savepoints), point-in-time recovery, native CSV import, direct ALTER COLUMN TYPE, major write buffer redesign for performance, and first native Win32 port. Slony-I replication also mentioned.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/92727/][TCP window scaling and broken routers]]: Some routers/firewalls mangle TCP window scaling options on SYN packets, causing mismatched window sizes between systems; 2.6.7's default scale factor of 7 exposed broken routers; David Miller rejected workaround patches, arguing router vendors should fix their broken equipment.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/92617/][Cryptographic signatures on kernel modules]]: Greg Kroah-Hartman and David Howells' GPG-based module signing implementation; signatures embedded as ELF sections; requires in-kernel DSA verification and arbitrary-precision integer math from GnuPG; Fedora Rawhide kernels include this feature to prevent loading corrupted modules.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/93574/][NULL v. zero]]: Sparse complaints about using 0 for null pointers triggered debate; Linus declared using NULL for pointers and 0 for integers official kernel style; argument is that NULL makes code intent clearer and enables better type checking.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/93604/][Addressing latency problems in 2.6]]: Multimedia latency issues addressed via Ingo Molnar/Arjan van de Ven voluntary preemption patch adding cond_reschedule() to might_sleep(); preempt kernel option not shipped by distributors due to stability fears; identified sources: console scrolling, ReiserFS, Ext3, driver ioctls with BKL held; xrun_debug helps ALSA users track latency.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/93617/][RCU-safe reference counting]]: Ravikiran G Thirumalai's refcount_t using cmpxchg() for atomic increment while checking count != 0; avoids race between kref_get/put; works with RCU callback for safe object deletion after quiescence; claimed 13-21% improvement for file descriptor lookups.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/95334/][Voluntary preemption and hardware interrupts]]: Ingo Molnar's voluntary preemption now moves hardware interrupt handling into process context via irqd (renamed ksoftirqd); claims 100μs max latency; timer interrupts still handled immediately; IDE completion made preemptable.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/95347/][A kernel events layer]]: Robert Love/Arjan van de Ven's send_kevent() API broadcasting events via netlink to user space for D-BUS integration; supports KEVENT_GENERAL, STORAGE, POWER, FS, HOTPLUG types; simple string-based event format.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/97154/][Safe seeks]]: Linus's patches for 2.6.8 add FMODE_LSEEK and separate pread/pwrite mode bits to VFS; nonseekable_open() helper marks objects non-seekable at open() time; sysctl API changed to pass offset explicitly; read/write now pass a copy of f_pos to methods, eliminating races.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/98463/][The end of the fcntl() method]]: fcntl() method removed from file_operations in 2.6.8, replaced with check_flags() for F_SETFL validation and dir_notify() for CIFS F_NOTIFY; untested NFS changes caused kernel crash requiring 2.6.8.1 quick release.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/99232/][Looking at reiser4]]: Reiser4 added to -mm; benchmarks showed ~33MB ext3 overhead vs near-zero for reiser4, 15% less space for kernel tree; performance mixed; concerns: fails with 4K kernel stacks, sys_reiser4() yacc parser controversial, O_DIRECTORY semantics broken allowing non-directories, locking problems caused DOS vulnerabilities.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/100098/][Pointless ideology?]]: Discusses removal of PWC webcam driver after author requested it when Greg KH removed binary module hook; rebuts criticism of "licensing fundamentalism" with practical reasons against proprietary modules: GPL compliance, platform lock-in, bug opacity, stability drag on kernel development; quotes Goldman Sachs tech manager on advantages of in-tree drivers at 2004 Kernel Summit.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/101210/][Figuring out kernel event reporting]]: Robert Love's kevent patch evolved: now uses kobject for sysfs object paths, dropped descriptive string; debates continue on type filtering necessity, signal as attribute vs verb, and unification with kobject_hotplug(); netlink favored by desktop but unavailable early boot; may need hybrid /sbin/hotplug + netlink approach.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/101230/][Kswapd and high-order allocations]]: Kswapd only checks free page count, not contiguity; Nick Piggin's patch keeps kswapd running until higher-order allocations possible; reverse mapping could enable targeted page coalescing but Linus prefers not to change page replacement policy; better approach may be driver scatter/gather DMA to avoid high-order allocation needs.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/103230/][An introduction to SELinux]]: NSA's SELinux uses Mandatory Access Control with Type Enforcement (TE) assigning types to kernel resources; RBAC associates users with roles which map to types; access vectors store allowed/auditallow/auditdeny permissions checked after normal Linux DAC; Fedora Core 2, Debian, Gentoo included it; complexity concerns noted—Fedora Core 3 ratcheted down default checking.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/104179/][Respite from the OOM killer]]: Thomas Habets' oom_pardon patch exempts processes from OOM killer; SUSE patch allows OOM score adjustment; explains Linux memory overcommit model where fork() uses COW so actual memory reserved is much less; Andries Brouwer's airplane crash analogy; disable OOM with vm.overcommit_memory=2.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/104343/][Watching filesystem events with inotify]]: John McCutchan/Robert Love's inotify replaces dnotify with /dev/inotify device and ioctl calls; INOTIFY_WATCH/IGNORE manage watching; supports many event types (access, modify, open/close, create/delete, rename); read/poll for events, no signals; nonotify exists as alternative; concerns about fd vs filename in interface.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/105021/][Active memory defragmentation]]: Marcelo Tosatti's patch for active defragmentation to solve high-order allocation failures; relocates pages by copying content and updating page tables using reverse mapping; skips locked, reserved, writeback pages; overlaps with hotplug memory work (IBM/Fujitsu patches) which also moves pages to free memory blocks.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/105366/][When should a process be migrated?]]: Kenneth Chen found 2.5ms cache_hot_time causes 12% throughput loss vs 10ms; Ingo Molnar's self-tuning approach measures actual cost of cache migration per system (5ms on P4, 87ms on P3 Xeon); argues one default doesn't fit all hardware.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/106010/][Approaches to realtime Linux]]: MontaVista's realtime patch threads interrupt handlers, replaces spinlocks with blocking mutexes with priority inheritance; Ingo Molnar improved it using existing semaphores with raw_spinlock_t for core locks (~90 remain); RTAI/Fusion runs Linux under Adeos hypervisor for hard realtime with user-space tasks; notes true hard realtime requires deterministic guarantees not just low latency.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/106177/][Four-level page tables]]: Andi Kleen's patch extends Linux's 3-level page tables to 4 levels for x86-64; adds PML4 (512-entry) enabling 128TB address space vs 512GB limit before; architectures without 4-level support emulate via default PML4 optimized out; headed for -mm tree.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/108216/][The ongoing realtime story]]: Ingo's -RT patches make more kernel code preemptible including memory allocators, timers, wait queues; converts semaphores to completions; Paul McKenney proposed dedicated realtime CPU via isolcpus= to avoid kernel lock contention; realtime preemption remains controversial due to scope of changes.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/108595/][Crash dumps with kexec]]: Kexec-based crash dump in -mm loads secondary kernel into reserved memory before crash; on panic, boots into crash dump kernel preserving full memory state of failed kernel; /proc/vmcore exposes old memory as ELF core dump, /dev/oldmem for raw access; crash kernel writes dump to disk then reboots.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/109458/][Schedulers, pluggable and realtime]]: Con Kolivas' plugsched framework splits sched.c into public scheduler.c and private per-scheduler code with struct sched_drv function pointers; Ingo Molnar opposed pluggable schedulers arguing they'd prevent cross-cutting work like scheduling domains; Ingo's -RT patches add priority inheritance to semaphores so high-priority tasks don't wait on lower-priority lock holders.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/109319/][Freeing the firmware]]: Vendors save cost by omitting device ROM and requiring host download; firmware redistribution often prohibited by restrictive licenses; OpenBSD's Theo de Raadt leading effort to pressure vendors; Atmel released firmware under BSD license; community asked to contact Intel/TI about making firmware redistributable for out-of-box OS support.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/111262/][Book review: SELinux]]: Review of Bill McCarty's "SELinux: NSA's Open Source Security Enhanced Linux"; thin but valuable for explaining SELinux concepts (roles, types, domain transitions), installation on Debian/Fedora/Gentoo, administration, RBAC/TE policy language; criticized for typos, contradictions, hasty production; missing guidance on designing policies from scratch.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/112396/][Should FUSE be merged?]]: FUSE allows filesystems in user space via daemon passing VFS requests; merger requested for 2.6; Linus called it "too messy" (some from old 2.4 code); critical deadlock: full memory with dirty pages to FUSE filesystem while user-space daemon is swapped out creates circular dependency on VM; shared writable mappings trigger it; solution: disallow shared writable mappings, limit queued write pages; Linus left door open for merger.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/113349/][Kernel headers and user space]]: David Howells' RFC proposes include/user directories for user-space-usable kernel header definitions; would separate __KERNEL__ code into separate files; Linus skeptical of wholesale reorganization, prefers fixing specific problematic headers incrementally rather than breaking existing users for aesthetic gains.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/113739/][Security (Weekly Update)]]: Matt Bishop's "Introduction to Computer Security" review: 750-page academic text covering protection models, MAC, cryptography, integrity/confidentiality policies, malicious logic, assurance, network security; new vulnerabilities: imlib buffer overflows (CAN-2004-1026), rssh/scponly unrestricted command execution, hpsockd overflow, mirrorselect temp file flaw.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/114596/][Improving page fault scalability]]: Christoph Lameter's patches address page_table_lock contention on many-CPU systems; uses ptep_cmpxchg() with atomic cmpxchg instead of lock for PTE changes; RSS counters in mm_struct caused cache line bouncing—tried atomic_t (expensive), sloppy_rss (~1% error), page table scanning (too slow); Linus suggested per-task RSS counters summed on query, avoiding lock contention; mm_struct freeing requires RCU; patch ready after 2.6.10, blocked by four-level page table conflicts.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/115405/][Debugfs]]: Greg Kroah-Hartman's debugfs provides lightweight virtual filesystem for debugging info, simpler than creating /proc files, sysfs (too rigid), or libfs filesystems; has debugfs_create_dir(), debugfs_create_file(), debugfs_create_u8/u16/u32/bool helpers; files must be explicitly removed with debugfs_remove() when module unloads; may gain kobject integration later.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/115554/][Boot-time clock frequency selection]]: Andrea Arcangeli's patch transforms HZ from compile-time constant to boot-time variable (touches 143 files); USER_HZ constant isolates user-space interfaces from variable kernel HZ; jiffies_to_clock_t() and user_to_kernel_hz() handle conversions; enables power savings on laptops and lower latency for interactive use; likely to be merged eventually.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/115651/][ioctl(), the big kernel lock, and 32-bit compatibility]]: Mike Werner's patch adds unlocked_ioctl() to file_operations, called without BKL; Michael Tsirkin's competing patch adds ioctl_native() and ioctl_compat() methods for clean 32/64-bit separation and avoiding global command table races; solves BKL problem plus 32-bit compat issues more comprehensively.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/115765/][Porting free software to Windows]]: KDE developers debate whether porting OSS to Windows helps or hurts Linux adoption; Aaron Seigo argues ports reduce migration incentive by making Windows have "best of both worlds"; others argue it bridges the gap for gradual adoption and respects user choice; organizations can use "smoother transition" approach; author concludes restricting user choice contradicts open source values.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/116810/][Rethinking four-level page tables]]: Nick Piggin posted alternative 4-level page table placement—new PUD level below PGD vs Andi's PML4 at top; Linus prefers Nick's approach as less intrusive (code not using new level can ignore it); Andi wants to stick with current patches as they've been tested longer in -mm; four-level tables might not make 2.6.11.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/117749/][Four-level page tables merged]]: Nick Piggin's PUD placement (below PGD) accepted for 2.6.11; x86-64 now has 128TB virtual address space vs 512GB before; demonstrates faster kernel development without distributor backports; peer review improved original Andi Kleen patch into less intrusive form.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/118750/][Circular pipes]]: Linus's circular buffer implementation replaces single-page pipe buffer; page array with nrbufs counter; 30-90% bandwidth improvement; future splice() and tee() system calls for zero-copy data transfer; tee enables splitting streams (video capture + display + file write simultaneously); single-byte writes currently use 16 pages—no coalescing yet (breaks kernel compiles).
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/118785/][Merging the realtime security module]]: Audio/JACK community needs low latency; realtime LSM grants CAP_SYS_NICE/IPC_LOCK/SYS_RESOURCE to special GID; memory locking solved via rlimits in 2.6.9; nice levels improved in 2.6.10 but SCHED_FIFO still required; generalized capability module proposed; SELinux never mentioned in context.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/118835/][The abrupt un-exporting of symbols]]: Paul McKenney requested restore of files_lock and set_fs_root() for IBM's proprietary MVFS module; kernel devs removed exports citing VFS internals shouldn't be used outside kernel; Linus criticized abrupt breaking without notice; feature-removal-schedule.txt created in Documentation/; out-of-tree module maintainers still face difficulties.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/118922/][Review: Linux Application Development, Second Edition]]: Review of LAD 2nd ed by Michael K. Johnson and Erik W. Troan; updated for 2.6 kernel and glibc 2.3; adds epoll, security pitfalls chapter, IPv6 sockets, regex, real-time signals; remains definitive low-level Linux C API reference despite some hasty updates and missing clone() flags, mmap() flags; web content search promised at ladweb.net.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/119652/][The new way of ioctl()]]: Michael Tsirkin's unlocked_ioctl() and compat_ioctl() added to file_operations; unlocked_ioctl removes BKL (inode available via filp->f_dentry->d_inode); compat_ioctl handles 32-bit on 64-bit without BKL; HAVE_UNLOCKED_IOCTL and HAVE_COMPAT_IOCTL macros for conditional compilation; Andi Kleen adding compat_ioctl to block_device_operations and scsi_host_template.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/119682/][The evolution of pipe buffers]]: pipe_buf_operations struct with can_merge flag (for coalescing writes), map/unmap/release methods; enables direct device memory windows in pipes; Linus's initial splice() syscall: sys_splice(fdin, fdout, len, flags) for pipe-to-pipe transfer; splice_write/splice_read file_operations methods; generic splice_read for filesystem-backed fds; generic implementation populates page cache then loads pages into pipe buffer.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/120850/][A new core time subsystem]]: John Stultz's timesource refactoring; separates architecture-specific time code into timesource_t struct (read_fnct, mmio_ptr, mask, mult, shift); common timeofday core handles NTP in separate file, works in nanoseconds independent of jiffies; register_timesource() API for time source drivers; supports TIMESOURCE_FUNCTION, TIMESOURCE_CYCLES, TIMESOURCE_MMIO_32/64 types; concerns: gettimeofday in user space not yet supported, NTP cost too high.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/121618/][Yet another approach to memory fragmentation]]: Mel Gorman's buddy allocator enhancement adds GFP_ flags (__GFP_USERRCLM for reclaimable user pages, __GFP_KERNRCLM for reclaimable kernel caches, non-marked = non-reclaimable) and separate free_area arrays per type; free_area_global holds MAX_ORDER blocks initially; each type's free_area starts empty, gets split chunks; coalescing stays within type-specific arrays until largest size returns to global; segregating pinned kernel pages makes rest easier to manage; vanilla kernel: 3/160 order-10 allocations succeeded; patched: 81/160 succeeded; active freeing not yet implemented.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/122477/][Audio latency goes full circle]]: Ingo's rlimit approach to realtime scheduling pulled from -mm due to abuse potential by proprietary apps and need for 100% CPU; Ingo apologized, says LSM solution is "least intrusive" and will be merged; problem: users need audio group GID, but GTK+ toolkit prevents setgid binaries from running, so users get more privilege than strictly needed.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/122579/][read() and write() access checking]]: Linus restored access_ok() checks at sys_read/write entry for 2.6.11; 2.0 had buffer writable check before VFS call, removed in 2.2; low-level checks only verify range actually used, missing bugs when requested range exceeds file size; entry-point check ensures all cases caught; low-level drivers should still keep access_ok() as it's cheap.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/124747/][FUTEX + rwsem = SNAFU]]: Olof Johansson found deadlock: FUTEX takes mmap_sem read, get_user() page faults and tries mmap_sem read again, but writer waiting causes sleep - deadlock; solution: new get_user_inatomic() function that's atomic (won't sleep, returns error instead); inc_preempt_count() before call, drop and restart on fault; eventual solution may differ but follows this approach.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/125882/][A day in the life of emacs]]: State of GNU Emacs development (2005): RMS remains active final decision maker; XEmacs manual reuse request denied—GNU Emacs manual is GFDL, incompatible with XEmacs GPL; new SXEmacs fork announced by Steve Youngs; Emacs 22 features: bundled Leim/Lisp manual, Cygwin/Mac OS X/S390 support, GTK+, URL clicking behavior, improved gnus/SQL/MH-E/cc modes, drag-drop, mouse wheel default, new conf-mode/dns-mode/flymake/thumbs/cua modes.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/125488/][A proposal for a major memory management rework]]: Christoph Lameter's MMU abstraction to replace hierarchical page tables in core kernel; mmu_entry_t (translation like PTE), mmu_translation_set_t (address space), mmu_transaction_t (atomic changes); initially on top of existing 4-level tables; would enable simpler huge page handling, global pages across processes; kernel devs want code not design proposals—discussion muted.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/125621/][Removing exported symbols in a stable kernel]]: Adrian Bunk's patch to unexport do_settimeofday() pulled after Andrew Morton demanded new policy: deprecate symbols first, add to feature-removal-schedule.txt, remove after one year; ARM has out-of-tree RTC modules needing do_settimeofday(); abrupt removals may be over.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/125831/][Toward the merging of Xen]]: Xen virtual machine merge blocked by Andi Kleen objecting to separate arch/xen duplicating i386 code; Andrew Morton agreed with concerns about maintenance burden; Ian Pratt proposed incremental approach: merge Xen into i386 first (halving modified files), then add as i386 subarchitecture with common parts in drivers/xen/core; approach seems to satisfy critics.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/126808/][A unified device number allocator]]: Matt Mackall's register_dev() unifies char/block device allocation; simple linked list sorted by device number; replaces duplicated code between char/block; function signature: register_dev(base, top, size, name, list, ret); linear search fine since allocation rare; dynamic major still 254 descending; register_chrdev/register_blkdev kept for compatibility; may eventually deprecate old interfaces.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/127669/][HALs considered harmful]]: Neterion/S2io 10GbE driver rejected for using proprietary HAL (xge_list_t, xge_os_spin_lock, etc.); David Miller: "HAL is unacceptable for in-tree drivers"; problems: performance overhead at high packet rates, maintainability (drivers look unlike Linux code), vendor abandonment risk, prevents community patches; Neterion reluctantly agrees to write non-HAL driver if required; other vendors may skip Linux.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/127698/][Handling interrupts in user space]]: Peter Chubb's user-space interrupt via /proc/irq/IRQ/irq with read/poll; handler disables IRQ, increments count, wakes waiting process; can't work with shared IRQs (disabling affects all devices); Alan Cox proposes passing IRQ descriptor so kernel can identify device and re-enable; SGI's ULI (ia64 only) uses assembly magic for direct user-space return from interrupt for better performance.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/129511/][Realtime preemption and read-copy-update]]: Ingo's RT patch conflicted with RCU because rcu_read_lock() disables preemption; Paul McKenney's solution: per-CPU count of processes in RCU critical sections tracked via current->rcu_data pointer and nesting counter; when nesting hits zero, decrement per-CPU count; per-CPU count zero = CPU quiesced; allows preemptible RCU critical sections while preserving scalability for non-RT config.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/130506/][Time for a new semaphore type?]]: Trond Myklebust's iosem for NFSv4 async locking needs; iosem_lock_and_schedule_work() schedules callback when lock available instead of blocking; replaces 20+ arch-specific semaphore implementations with single generic version; iosem_work struct with callback function; Benjamin LaHaise prefers rationalizing existing semaphores instead.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/130696/][The kernel and binary firmware]]: request_firmware() exists but too simple for complex firmware (tg3 has executable sections with metadata); needs extension for firmware with multiple sections; firmware often required in initrd/initramfs at boot; Debian considers kernel+binary blob as "mere aggregation" not derived work if blob license permits redistribution; infrastructure for user-space firmware loading still incomplete.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/131657/][The guts of git]]: Git = low-level object database + directory cache, not full SCM; objects: blobs (file contents), trees (directory state), commits (history points); SHA-1 addressed; write-tree creates tree from cache, commit-tree adds history; git-pasky adds SCM layer; Andrew tested: 198 patches applied in ~3 min, 9MB from 859KB mbox; growth ~2GB/year; rsync for network transfer.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/131856/][FUSE hits a snag]]: FUSE presents tar archive permissions directly (tarfs disables normal permission checking) so mounter sees files as owned by archive contents; kernel devs want VFAT-style behavior where mounter owns files; Miklos Szeredi argues FUSE is single-user by design (like running tar); allow_others option exists but raises security questions; namespace approach proposed but complex.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/132796/][A very quick guide to starting with git]]: Steps to try git: grab git-pasky from pasky.or.cz, make, git pull pasky, make; create kernel tree dir, git init rsync://kernel.org/.../linux-2.6.git to pull repo; git log shows commit history, git pull updates; git alone lists commands; format changed once already requiring repo migration; kernel starts clean slate without BK history (too big).
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/132196/][An introduction to KProbes]]: IBM's kernel debugging tool; KProbe = pre-handler + post-handler at instruction address; JProbe = access function arguments via handler with same prototype, uses setjmp/longjmp; register_kprobe/unregister_kprobe; kprobe struct with addr, handlers, fault_handler; stored in hash table protected by kprobe_lock; x86 uses int3 breakpoint, single-step execution; preemption/interrupts disabled during probe handling; recursion detected, probe silently disabled; fault_handler can return 0 to let kernel handle or 1 if handled.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/133649/][Supporting RDMA on Linux]]: RDMA pins user pages long-term but get_user_pages() has no resource accounting or privilege checking; hostile app could lock memory indefinitely; no process-exit notification; COW fork breaks RDMA buffers; solutions: require mlock(), VM_DONTCOPY on RDMA VMA; proposed ioproc_ops hooks into VM: release(), sync_range/page(), invalidate_range/page(), update_range/page(), change_protection(); driver registers via ioproc_register_ops() on mm_struct; alternative: card has own MMU, kernel helps sync page tables when host VM changes.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/133848/][FUSE and private namespaces]]: MNT_PRIVATE flag rejected—setuid would see different fs than normal processes; namespace solution: FUSE mounts hidden from other users via per-process namespace; chroot via /proc/*pid*/root accesses other process's namespace; privileged daemon could pass namespace fd to processes via fd passing; could hide in PAM module; FUSE problem is really "user mount problem" not FUSE-specific.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/134513/][Defending against fork bombs]]: Coywolf Qi Hunt's OOM killer patch tracks "biological" parent/child separately; when second OOM victim chosen, finds least common ancestor via parent pointers and kills ancestor + all children; effectively eliminates fork bomb process trees; tested with fork bombs, recovered with some collateral damage; adds pointer + 2 list_head overhead per process.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/135472/][Execute-in-place]]: Carsten Otte's XIP patches add direct_access to block_device_operations, get_xip_page to address_space_operations (ext2 implemented), filemap_xip_nopage replaces nopage for XIP; page tables point directly to on-disk pages avoiding RAM copy; David Woodhouse noted alternative is special filesystem; debate: flash manufacturers push XIP but RAM cheaper and compression more efficient; XIP useful for fast boot and shared-memory block devices.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/136273/][Is hyperthreading dangerous?]]: Colin Percival's cache timing attack via HT: measure memory access latency to infer what sibling thread accessed; can steal crypto keys from OpenSSL via data-dependent memory access patterns; mitigations: disable HT (noht boot option), scheduler isolation, remove high-res timers, rewrite crypto to avoid data-dependent access; Linux unlikely to change—exploit requires running unnoticed on same HT chip, noisy channel, timing difficulties; dual-core less vulnerable (shares L2 not L1).
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/136425/][A new kernel timer API]]: Nishanth Aravamudan's nanosecond timer API built on John Stultz's time subsystem; set_timer_nsecs() takes absolute nsec_t expires, set_timer_on_nsecs() sets on specific CPU; schedule_timeout_nsecs/usecs/msecs for relative delays; replaces jiffies with do_monotonic_clock() returning nanoseconds; deprecated: add_timer(), mod_timer(), schedule_timeout(); expires field format changed—old code doing math on expires may break.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/138174/][The beginning of the realtime preemption debate]]: Ingo Molnar's RT preempt patches convert spinlocks to rt_mutex with priority inheritance, thread interrupt handlers, make RCU/seqlocks preemptible; audio community already running -RT kernels with few bugs; opposition: too intrusive (~18K lines), small user base, performance penalty for all even when disabled; alternatives: RTAI-Fusion nanokernel (small verified core, Linux runs as user process) or Iguana/L4 microkernel; Ingo may push for piecemeal merge of individual features.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/138242/][A Look at The Onion Router (Tor)]]: Anonymous communication system by Nick Mathewson/Roger Dingledine (EFF-funded); routes through onion routers each knowing only adjacent nodes; runs as client, server (exit node), or hidden services; best used with Privoxy/tsocks/ProxyChains; notable performance lag for browsing; exit node traffic unencrypted—malicious exit node could monitor traffic; potential JavaScript issues, traffic analysis possible.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/138982/][Intel processors and DRM]]: Intel VP clarifies no "secret DRM" in chips—DTCP-IP is 5C Entity technology for protected home network content transport (IEEE1394, wireless); uses signed device certificates, encryption, revocation lists; devices authenticate before exchanging encrypted content with copy flags; hardware attestation ensures approved devices; not about locking out unauthorized OSes.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/139047/][A summary of the realtime Linux discussion]]: Paul McKenney's comprehensive summary: (1) non-CONFIG_PREEMPT: stock, 10s of ms latency, full performance; (2) CONFIG_PREEMPT: preemptible kernel, 100s of microseconds; (3) CONFIG_PREEMPT_RT: Ingo's patch, ~10 microseconds, rt_mutex, priority inheritance, ~18K diff; (4) Nested OS: Linux under RTOS, excellent latency but two OSes to admin; (5) Dual-OS/Dual-Core: RTOS + Linux on separate cores; (6) Migration Between OSes: tasks transparently migrate between RTOS and Linux; (7) Migration Within OS: designate realtime CPUs, migrate non-deterministic ops to other CPUs; no single solution fits all needs.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/139923/][MD5 collisions]]: MD5 broken: Dan Kaminsky's Stripwire creates executables with same MD5; Vlastimil Klima found collisions in 8 hours on 1.6GHz notebook; Lenstra/Wang/de Weger created colliding X.509 certificates; Lucks/Daum created two PostScript docs with same MD5 (recommendation letter vs access order) using Wang/Yu attack; builds can be compromised to embed dormant payloads activated later; affected: Tripwire, DRM, package managers; solution: use SHA-256/SHA-512; GPG signatures safe due to random prepend; should-sign policies should reject non-plaintext formats.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/139784/][Realtime and interrupt latency]]: Separate from scheduling latency, interrupt latency matters for hard realtime; biggest source: local_irq_disable(); with RT patches spinlocks don't spin so no deadlock risk, no need to disable interrupts for that; Daniel Walker's patch: local_irq_disable() now disables preemption instead, leaving interrupt hardware enabled; hard_local_irq_disable() for true hardware interrupt disable; Ingo merged it. Karim Yaghmour asked why not Adeos—Adeos interrupt pipeline with priority domains, higher priority handlers called first; Adeos benchmarks showed better latency than RT patches in some tests. Gerrit Huizenga argued RT must benefit average users or remain "ugly stepchild."
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/139964/][A look at Xen]]: Xen hypervisor/VMM requires OS ports unlike VMware full emulation; superior performance; runs Linux, NetBSD, FreeBSD but not unmodified Windows (yet); ports: x86_64, IA64 (HP), Power5 (IBM); FC4 and SUSE 9.3 include Xen; setup requires manual config, no GUI; web control interface works on live CD; can migrate VMs between machines with ~tens of milliseconds downtime; pre-copy approach: dirty pages transferred while guest runs, then stop-and-copy for remainder; Quake3 server migration ~60ms downtime, Apache 300ms without dropped clients. Xen 3.0 roadmap includes SMP for guests. XenSource founding with funding from KPCB; proprietary tools planned but hypervisor stays open source.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/170954/][Asynchronous I/O and vectored operations]]: Christoph Hellwig/Badari Pulavarty's vectored AIO patch: aio_read/aio_write now take iovec array instead of single buffer; single-buffer callers use single-entry iovec; readv/writev methods eliminated—filesystems must implement aio_read/aio_write for vectored I/O; if only AIO methods provided, read/readv calls fallback to aio_read; eventually all I/O paths could be async per 2003 Kernel Summit vision; large patch ripples through all filesystems/drivers.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/170822/][Software suspend - again]]: swsusp vs Suspend2 debate continues; Pavel Machek controls current swsusp, resistant to Suspend2; Andrew Morton suggested tossing both and implementing "suspend3" via kdump infrastructure—boot into dump kernel avoiding complex process freezing; kdump-based suspend would need ~8-16MB reserved for suspend kernel; Nigel Cunningham: kexec can't support file-backed suspend-to-ram, user complexity issues, may need 200-300MB for 1GB image restore; Eric Biederman notes 8-16MB enough for glibc-based user space; debate unresolved.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/171017/][PID virtualization: a wealth of choices]]: Two container PID virtualization approaches: (1) OpenVZ VPS: real and virtual PIDs, specific bit marks virtual PIDs, convert functions between them, fork can create with specific virtual PID; (2) Eric Biederman's pspace: each process has "wait ID" (how parent knows it), processes in same pspace share PID range, clone() flag creates new space, kill/find_task_by_pid take pspace param; Biederman: incremental namespace approach, no real/virtual distinction; OpenVZ: host can gdb/strace/kill container processes, existing tools work; Linus likes container concept but not necessarily OpenVZ PID approach.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/171941/][Tweaks to madvise() and posix_fadvise()]]: Michael Tsirkin's MADV_DONTFORK flag for madvise() sets VM_DONTCOPY so forked children don't see that memory region—solves COW breaking parent-driver page sharing when driver uses get_user_pages(); MADV_DOFORK cancels it. Andrew Morton added posix_fadvise() options: LINUX_FADV_ASYNC_WRITE starts write I/O on given range, LINUX_FADV_WRITE_WAIT waits for I/O in progress; replaces lost msync(MS_ASNC) 2.4 semantics where 2.6 delays actual I/O; expect 2.6.17 timeframe.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/172149/][Robust futexes - a new approach]]: Ingo Molnar/Thomas Gleixner/Ulrich Drepper's new robust futex solution—maintains user-space linked list of held futexes per thread; set_robust_list(head, size) syscall registers list location with kernel; on exit, kernel walks list carefully, releases locks, marks them with special value indicating previous holder died improperly, wakes waiters; glibc maintains list; race handled via "pending" field checked on exit; max 1M locks released prevents infinite loops; enables standardized robust pthread_mutexattr_setrobust_np(); glibc modifications already written by Drepper.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/172844/][The kevent interface]]: Evgeniy Polyakov's kevent subsystem with 3 new syscalls; unifies event handling for sockets, poll events, inotify, timers, async network I/O; kevent_ctl(fd, ctl) with cmd: KEVENT_CTL_INIT/ADD/REMOVE/MODIFY/WAIT; ukevent struct with id, type (KEVENT_SOCKET/INODE/POLL/TIMER/NAIO), event flags, req_flags; aio_send/aio_recv for zero-copy async network I/O; proto structure gets async_recv/async_send methods; only TCP implemented; user-space interface criticized as needing work before mainline merge.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/172986/][Sysfs and a stable kernel ABI]]: Removing mount/unmount user events broke iPod support via HAL; Andrew Morton/Linus rebuke: "Don't. Do. That." policy against breaking user-space ABI; feature removal schedule added with Feb 2007 removal date; sysfs has 3400 attributes in 1175 directories—all part of kernel ABI; sysfs tied to kernel data structures creates long-term constraint on internal API changes; risk of forcing compatibility translation layers in future.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/152436/][A new approach to kernel timers]]: Thomas Gleixner's ktimers from 2.6.13-rt6; separates timeouts (low-res, usually removed before expiring) from timers (high-res, usually expire); current timer wheel structure: tv1-tv5 arrays with cascade operations, efficient for timeouts but tied to jiffies; ktimers use nanoseconds, per-CPU sorted red-black trees, no cascade needed; init_ktimer_mono() for monotonic clock, init_ktimer_real() for realtime clock; start_ktimer modes: KTIMER_ABS/REL/INCR/FORWARD/REARM/RESTART; schedule_ktimer() for process sleep; standalone patch runs on normal clock interrupt, separate arch-specific patch enables high-res on i386; objection: nanoseconds require 64-bit vars on 32-bit systems.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/151624/][Mercurial: an alternative to git]]: Matt Mackall's Mercurial (hg) distributed SCM written in Python with C extensions; ~7500 lines; uses revlogs (delta compressed revision logs) stored per-file reducing disk seeks vs git's SHA1-based naming; push/pull development model; HTTP/SSH sync; Xen project uses it; hg pull/push commands familiar to CVS/SVN users; interoperates with git via repository tracking Linus's tree; faster on some operations but git pack files often smaller; similar feature sets but Mercurial CLI more consistent.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/149036/][Guten Tag from Avahi]]: Avahi 0.1 released—zeroconf/mDNS/DNS-SD service discovery framework using same specs as Apple's Bonjour; LGPL licensed; D-BUS API with Python/Mono bindings; not GNOME or KDE specific though developers are GNOME people; avahi-dnsconfd configures unicast DNS via mDNS for IPv6; only Linux currently due to netlink BSD-compatibility issues; fully usable with some optional mDNS "SHOULDs" unimplemented.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/146689/][Toward more robust network-based block I/O]]: Daniel Phillips' patch for iSCSI/nfs root deadlock when memory pressure causes writing dirty pages over network requiring more memory for packets; adjust_memalloc_reserve() lets drivers claim memory for packet receive; __GFP_MEMALLOC flag for emergency allocations; dev_memalloc_skb() uses reserve; SOCK_MEMALLOC socket flag marks block I/O sockets entitled to use reserves; non-blockio packets from competing traffic dropped when reserves are depleted; self-correcting since competing tasks block waiting for memory.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/146861/][A realtime preemption overview]]: Paul McKenney's comprehensive PREEMPT_RT overview: minimizes non-preemptible code; spinlock_t/rwlock_t now preemptible with priority inheritance via rt_mutex; RCU read-side preemptible; interrupt handlers run in process context except SA_NODELAY (timer tick only); local_irq_disable() now disables preemption not hardware irqs; raw_spinlock_t for true hardware irq disable in low-level code; compat_semaphore/rw_semaphore avoid priority inheritance for event-style usage; deferred operations (put_task_struct_delayed, mmdrop_delayed); TIF_NEED_RESCHED_DELAYED defers reschedule when wakeup followed immediately by spin_unlock; reader-writer locks limited to one reader at a time for PI tractability.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/140333/][A couple of graphical git front ends]]: gitk (Tk/Tcl by Paul Mackerras) and qgit (Qt) for browsing git repositories; both are patch browsers only, can't modify repos; gitk simpler—single script, shows commit list with merge graph, file list, patch view, regex search; qgit uses SCons (bundled), shows nicer diff-uw format, line annotation showing which commit modified each line, filter search; both show patch ancestry clearly unlike BitKeeper.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/142751/][Looking at inotify again]]: Greg Kroah-Hartman's guide; /dev/inotify misc character device (mknod /dev/inotify c 10 63); ioctl INOTIFY_WATCH to add watch (fd, mask), INOTIFY_IGNORE to remove; masks: IN_ACCESS/MODIFY/ATTRIB/CLOSE_WRITE/CLOSE_NOWRITE/OPEN/MOVED_FROM/MOVED_TO/CREATE/DELETE/DELETE_SELF; read() returns inotify_event {wd, mask, cookie, len, name}; cookie synchronizes paired events like rename; supports select/poll; FIONREAD returns queue size; closing fd releases all watches; doesn't block unmounts like FAM/dnotify.
