- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/613260/][Mahinovs: Distributing third party applications via Docker?]]: Proposal for Docker-based application distribution where developers write Dockerfiles that compile applications into optimized runtime images, which can then be exported and distributed as single files containing filesystem layers and metadata. The approach sparked debate about security update mechanisms, repeatable builds, and how Docker-based distribution relates to traditional package management systems for enterprise deployments.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/613988/][LibreSSL: More Than 30 Days Later]]: LibreSSL development team led by Ted Unangst and Joel Sommer create ressl, a reimagined SSL API focused on simplicity and consistency by asking "what would the user like to do?" rather than "what does the TLS protocol allow?". The discussion highlighted that hostname checking and certificate verification are often neglected in TLS libraries, and better APIs should provide secure defaults while allowing explicit overrides for nonstandard configurations.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/614144/][Hyde: What's Wrong with Markdown]]: Adam Hyde critiques Markdown's design as a plain-text format meant to be readable without rendering, arguing that treating it primarily as an HTML generator contradicts its original purpose. He notes that structured text (headings, lists, paragraphs) is the basic requirement of any text format, making Markdown's claim to special status questionable. Commenters cited CommonMark standardization efforts as addressing some concerns, though the tension between readable plain-text and HTML generation remains.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/614421/][The Book of PF, 3rd edition -- New from No Starch Press]]: Third edition of the OpenBSD PF firewall guide by Peter N.M. Hansteen covers basics through advanced setups including IPv6 support and OpenBSD's new traffic-shaping system replacing ALTQ, aimed at FreeBSD and NetBSD users as well.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612869/][Reviving TTX and FontTools]]: Google's Behdad Esfahbod revived FontTools, a dormant Python font library, adding optimization and batch-processing tools for Google Fonts. TTX converts OpenType fonts to XML for easier editing while preserving all font data during round-trip conversion.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612829/][A Dark Room]]: Independent HTML5/JavaScript text adventure game by Michael Townsend that reached #1 on iOS App Store charts and was released under MPL open source to encourage learning and creativity in the developer community.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612837/][Adobe releases source code for OpenType font-development tools]]: Adobe open-sourced proprietary font tools at ATypI 2014, including donating CFF rasterizing engine to FreeType for mobile/browser support. Represents shift from closed specifications to open-source strategy for cross-platform font viability.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612818/][Schneier on incident response]]: Bruce Schneier's AppSec USA keynote on three security trends: loss of infrastructure control via cloud/mobile, increasingly sophisticated attacks, and IoT expanding attack surface. Analyzed attacker skill/focus taxonomy including APT threat models requiring fundamentally different defenses.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612451/][Simply Secure announces itself]]: New security usability organization launched by Google and Dropbox with Open Technology Fund, focusing on public audits of interfaces and code to validate security and usability claims of supported projects.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612963/][Security Collapse in the HTTPS Market (ACM Queue)]]: ACM Queue article analyzing systemic HTTPS vulnerabilities, CA market concentration ("too big to fail"), perverse incentives, and why proposed E.U. legislation reinforces rather than fixes market failures.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/613032/][A remotely exploitable hole in bash]]: Bash function export via environment variables allows arbitrary command execution after function definitions - bash continues parsing after the "() {" definition and executes trailing commands. Major attack vector through CGI scripts (Shellshock vulnerability).
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612483/][Non-blocking buffered file read operations]]: New readv2/writev2/preadv2/pwritev2 syscalls with RWF_NONBLOCK flag enable non-blocking behavior at the read() call level; returns EAGAIN if requested data is not already in the page cache.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612878/][The BPF system call API, version 14]]: BPF syscall (bpf()) provides map management (BPF_MAP_CREATE/Update/Lookup/Delete) and program loading via BPF_PROG_LOAD; the kernel verifier prevents harmful operations like arbitrary memory access and loops.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612960/][Hutterer: libinput - a common input stack for Wayland compositors and X.Org drivers]]: Peter Hutterer explains why the X.Org input stack is a mess and the new 'libinput' stack is needed. The X server tries to keep the peace between conflicting drivers like synaptics, evdev, wacom, and evdev needs to support multitouch while synaptics also wants to do that but they do it differently. libinput provides a unified input handling layer for both Wayland compositors and X.Org drivers.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612244/][Adding strlcpy() to glibc]]: Jonathan Corbet covers the long-running discussion about adding strlcpy() to glibc. The strlcpy() family of string functions originated in BSD to address security problems with strcpy(). Despite years of resistance due to concerns about string truncation handling, the functions address real-world buffer overflow vulnerabilities that older functions like strncpy() fail to prevent.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/611969/][X and SteamOS]]: Jake Edge reports on Keith Packard's talk 'SteamOS Magic' at LinuxCon North America. Valve took the existing X-based desktop and turned it into a living-room console experience with a fairly small amount of code. SteamOS uses a custom X server build with a gaming-focused interface overlay rather than a traditional desktop.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612015/][OpenSSL's new security policy]]: Nathan Willis covers OpenSSL's new public security policy describing how vulnerabilities will be dealt with. After the Heartbleed vulnerability, the Linux Foundation's Core Infrastructure Initiative funded work on OpenSSL to improve security processes and establish clear disclosure timelines. The policy aims to restore user confidence by formalizing how security holes are handled and communicated.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/611657/][Yao: The State of ZFS on Linux]]: Richard Yao at ClusterHQ blog argues that ZFSOnLinux (ZoL) is ready for production use. ZoL shares a common code base with other Open ZFS platforms, giving it the opportunity to rapidly implement features from the upstream Open ZFS project. The port enables native ZFS support directly in the Linux kernel without requiring FUSE.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612200/][Garrett: ACPI, kernels and contracts with firmware]]: Matthew Garrett discusses the challenges of ACPI support on ARM systems, noting that firmware is always buggy requiring workarounds, and raises questions about who will maintain the official Linux ACPI behavior test suite and whether ACPI is better than device tree for these systems.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612021/][Relativistic hash tables, part 1: Algorithms]]: Linux kernel 3.17 introduced relativistic hash tables that can be resized while lookups proceed concurrently, using RCU and clever pointer manipulation to "unzip" chains during expansion.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612100/][Relativistic hash tables, part 2: Implementation]]: Implementation details of relativistic hash tables covering how they handle concurrent lookups during table resizing operations using the RCU grace period mechanism.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612153/][The kernel address sanitizer]]: KASAN (Kernel Address Sanitizer) is a memory error detector that uses shadow memory to track memory accesses and detect out-of-bounds errors in the Linux kernel.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612110/][New MINIX release for x86 and ARM is BSD compatible]]: MINIX 3.3.0 released with improved BSD compatibility for both x86 and ARM architectures.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/611753/][Summary from the "State of the ARM" BoF at DC14]]: DebConf 14 BoF covering Debian ARM ports: armel (soft-float, ARMv4t+) is still maintained but other distros have dropped older ARM support, while armhf (hard-float, ARMv7+) is the current port; future plans include potentially dropping armel and reconsidering ARMv5 as a baseline given changes in upstream kernel and toolchain support.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612286/][Qubes: The Open Source OS Built for Security]]: Security-focused Qubes OS uses Xen hypervisor for compartmentalization, keeping qemu outside the trusted computing base and utilizing driver domains to sandbox networking and USB stacks.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/611772/][Klumpp: Listaller: Back to the future!]]: Matthias Klumpp announced a substantial Listaller rewrite to replace PackageKit's plugin infrastructure with AppStream integration; the new version will initially support only statically linked software with a minimal core, adding features like dependency-solving gradually only if useful.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612042/][The road to Rust 1.0]]: Rust blog describes recent ownership and borrowing changes that simplify the language by moving functionality to libraries rather than language constructs, with direct mapping to machine operations and no required runtime or external dependencies.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/611212/][Virgil 3D: A virtual GPU]]: Dave Airlie's open-source Virgil 3D project creates a virtual GPU for QEMU using virtio and Mesa to provide 3D acceleration for Linux guests on Linux hosts, competing with proprietary VMware and VirtualBox solutions.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/611236/][The KDE Visual Design Group]]: The KDE Visual Design Group (VDG) is a community-driven design team formed to bring open-source collaboration principles to visual design work. It combats the "lone genius" designer myth by encouraging inclusive participation regardless of skill level, and focuses on low-hanging fruit and responsive collaboration with developers to produce sustainable design outcomes for KDE.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/611155/][The OpenSSL security policy]]: OpenSSL published a security incident response policy document, stating they lack resources to maintain their own trusted vendor list and prefer to focus development effort on the code rather than managing security frameworks and vendor relationships.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/611226/][Slow probing + udev + SIGKILL = trouble]]: A conflict between systemd-udev's 30-second module loading timeout and kernel threads created by asynchronous storage driver probing caused boot failures when OOM killer sent SIGKILL to blocked kthread_create() calls. Luis Rodriguez proposed async probe patches adding an async_probe field to struct device_driver, but Tejun Heo opposed the approach, arguing it changes user-visible behavior and doesn't scale since any driver can exhibit this problem.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/610972/][Using RCU for linked lists — a case study]]: This case study explores incrementally converting Linux task list management from spinlocks to RCU (Read-Copy-Update), applying patterns like Existence Locks, Mark Obsolete Objects, and Ordered Update/Read to enable non-blocking concurrent access while maintaining correctness during list modifications.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/610975/][A common Markdown]]: CommonMark (originally "Standard Markdown") is a formal Markdown specification created by Jeff Atwood, John MacFarlane, and others to resolve ambiguities in Gruber's original format. After Gruber objected to the name implying official status, the project was renamed from "Standard Markdown" to "CommonMark," with reference implementations available in C and JavaScript under BSD license.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/611473/][New from Pragmatic Bookshelf: "Mastering Clojure Macros"]]: Pragmatic Bookshelf book on Clojure's macro system covering "code-as-data" philosophy, evaluating code in other contexts, moving computations to compile time, and creating clean API layers.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/611369/][Linux Audio Mini Summit 2014]]: Annual Linux audio developer meeting held alongside Linux Plumbers Conference in Düsseldorf for discussing low-level audio infrastructure including ALSA drivers and sound backends.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608732/][OPW and the GNOME mission]]: Outreach Program for Women (OPW) originally a GNOME project now administered by GNOME Foundation, helping women contribute to free and open-source software; may have outgrown its original home while administration and finances remain a strain.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608841/][Zooming with Eagle Mode]]: Eagle Mode is an open-source zoomable user interface (ZUI) file manager using zooming as the primary navigation metaphor, departing from traditional file managers like Nautilus and Dolphin for navigating complex graphical information.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/609048/][Packages, software centers, and AppData]]: AppData/AppStream specifications powering GNOME Software Center, addressing the mismatch where PackageKit treated package=application but they're not equivalent; Richard Hughes presented updates at Flock 2014.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/609003/][Security and boundaries]]: James Bottomley's 2014 Linux Security Summit keynote examined UEFI Secure Boot's security boundaries. He argued that Secure Boot's design places absolute trust in the platform owner while distrusting the OS, creating a fundamental conflict for Linux systems. The Linux solution (shim) subverts the Secure Boot root of trust—a necessity but "a really bad idea" for security systems without a better alternative.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608929/][Linux Kernel Git Repositories Add 2-Factor Authentication (Linux.com)]]: The Linux kernel git repositories now support two-factor authentication (2FA) via the kernel.org infrastructure. This addresses concerns about the security of kernel source code after past compromises. Developers can enable 2FA through the kernel.org identity management system.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608896/][An md/raid6 data corruption bug]]: A data corruption bug in Linux's md/raid6 software RAID implementation could cause incorrect parity calculations under specific fault conditions. The bug manifests when a write failure occurs during a reshape operation, potentially leading to silent data corruption in RAID6 arrays.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608900/][The 2014 Kernel Summit]]: The 2014 Kernel Summit brought together Linux kernel maintainers and developers to discuss technical roadmap items. Key topics included improving the kernel development process, scaling subsystem maintainership, and addressing technical challenges in memory management, networking, and file systems.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608914/][Error handling for I/O memory management units]]: Error handling for I/O memory management units (IOMMU) in Linux was discussed, focusing on how the kernel handles translation faults. The presentation examined the challenges of graceful error recovery when DMA transactions encounter invalid memory mappings, and potential approaches to improve robustness.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608917/][The stable tree]]: Greg Kroah-Hartman and Li Zefan led a 2014 Kernel Summit session on stable tree management. Most subsystems now properly route important patches to stable, though concerns remain about 200 patches marked for backporting in 3.17-rc1 that should go directly to mainline. Greg stepped down from managing the 3.4 LTS kernel, passing it to Li, and discussed whether stable should split into fixes-only versus new-device trees.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608933/][The state of linux-next]]: Stephen Rothwell reported that ~90% of mainline merge window code appears in linux-next first, from over 220 feeding trees. Some "preparatory trees" are unstable and get rebased frequently. ARM build testing was recently added (previously only x86/PowerPC), revealing that developers often submit code that has never been compiled, with allmodconfig tests catching this regularly.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608945/][Kernel tinification]]: Josh Triplett argued at the 2014 Kernel Summit that Linux has grown too large for embedded systems with limited flash and memory. The minimum kernel size increases with nearly every release, partly due to mandatory subsystems that can't be configured out and lack of size metrics. tiny.wiki.kernel.org documents efforts to build kernels an order of magnitude smaller.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608950/][What makes Linus happy (or not)?]]: Linus Torvalds discussed merge window preferences at the 2014 Kernel Summit, emphasizing that pull requests (marked with "pull" in subject) are prioritized over patch submissions, and subsystem maintainers should be timely. Merge commit messages have improved significantly. He expressed strong disapproval of dismissing regressions and forged email messages.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608954/][Kernel performance regressions]]: Facebook's Chris Mason discussed kernel performance regressions at the 2014 Kernel Summit, noting they maintain extensive performance metrics across production systems running 3.10-stable with ~75 additional patches. Despite fewer regressions than expected, they found a 10% IPv6 regression (already fixed upstream) and CPU frequency governor issues causing unwanted latencies, leading them to use the ACPI-based governor.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608959/][Kernel self tests]]: kselftest is a new make target in the Linux kernel build system introduced at the 2014 Kernel Summit by Shuah Khan for running basic sanity tests in under 15-20 minutes, aiming to catch regressions before stable releases. The project consolidates existing test code and addresses concerns about bisection difficulties when tests themselves change. Current tests run in well under ten minutes, with a goal of staying under 15-20 minutes to encourage developer use.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608968/][Two sessions on review]]: 2014 Kernel Summit sessions on code review highlighted the fundamental problem of insufficient reviewers, particularly for binary interfaces that must be maintained long-term. Testing by Michael Kerrisk found bugs in 50% of new system calls, with issues including missing ABI specifications and designs like O_TMPFILE that slipped through without adequate review. The lack of man pages for most new system calls compounds the problem of ensuring questionable designs are caught.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608992/][One year of Coverity work]]: Dave Jones ran Coverity scanner on the Linux kernel for a year using a kitchen-sink build with 6,955 configuration options, showing defect density decreased from 0.68 to 0.52 per thousand lines of code across kernels 3.11 to 3.17. The staging tree had the highest defect density, followed by driver code, with dead code and unchecked return values being the most common issues identified. Two to three scans are run daily on a dedicated server Coverity set up for kernel analysis.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608550/][Axiomatic validation of memory barriers and atomic instructions]]: Paul McKenney and Alan Stern explain memory barriers and atomic instructions using litmus tests, demonstrating that x86 can reorder stores before loads allowing both CPUs in a test to see zero. Atomic operations like atomic_inc prevent lost increments when multiple CPUs modify the same variable simultaneously. Memory barriers are needed because both compilers and CPUs can reorder memory references, with mfence instructions enforcing ordering on x86.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608915/][Consolidating server messages with fedmsg]]: Fedora's fedmsg is a decentralized messaging bus using ZeroMQ that unifies notifications from infrastructure services into uniformly formatted JSON messages with D-Bus-style topics, replacing heterogeneous message-generating packages across the Fedora ecosystem.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608665/][Riddell: Upstream and Downstream: why packaging takes time]]: Packaging KDE reveals C++ ABI compatibility challenges—symbol versioning must be maintained when libraries change, with libabigail helping detect breaks. Discussion also covered Rust as an alternative and tradeoffs between header-only templates versus binary compatibility.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608677/][GNOME development updates from GUADEC]]: GUADEC 2014 featured GNOME Continuous (hardware-based CI testing), GStreamer with OpenGL integration and new device monitor API, GOM object mapper for SQL databases, GeoClue geolocation updates, and Cockpit server management interface.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608761/][Ten years of OpenStreetMap]]: OpenStreetMap's ten-year growth to 1.5M users represents the most significant open geo data development since GPS. The project now focuses on detailed features like connected nodes, turn restrictions for navigation, and address geocoding rather than raw data collection.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/609070/][Fenzi: A week of gnome 3 from a Xfce using sysadmin]]: Kevin Fenzi's migration from Xfce to GNOME Shell highlighted limitations in session management (xscreensaver script incompatibility), systemd-logind now handling lid-close suspend, HiDPI display issues, and notification queue constraints.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/609042/][Libre Music Production]]: New community-driven web portal promoting FLOSS audio software for music creation, providing guides, tutorials, and hands-on material for new and non-technical users.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/609040/][Seven Concurrency Models in Seven Weeks]]: Pragmatic Bookshelf book covering parallelism and concurrency models including actors, functional programming, GPU exploitation, MapReduce, and Stream Processing to address multi-core and distributed system challenges.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/609041/][Metaprogramming Ruby 2]]: Pragmatic Bookshelf book explaining Ruby's metaprogramming capabilities in a practical style, providing tools for writing maintainable and flexible code.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608426/][Lessons from the Novena laptop project]]: Sean Cross's Flock 2014 keynote on the Novena open-hardware laptop featuring quad-core ARM Cortex A9, FPGA, and breakout connectors for add-ons; raised $720K via crowdfunding with all schematics publicly available.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608448/][Plasma 5: Evolving the KDE desktop]]: KDE Plasma 5.0 release introducing flat design, high-DPI support, converged shell for multiple device types, and KDE Frameworks 5 with 50+ portable libraries for cross-platform development.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608566/][Batsov: The State of Some Emacs Packages for Clojure Development]]: CIDER 0.7 is the new Clojure IDE for Emacs, replacing the deprecated clojure-emacs package set. It provides integrated REPL, debugger, and code completion for Clojure. The upgrade consolidates previously separate packages into a unified toolchain.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608491/][LPC: An In-Depth Look: Live Kernel Patching Microconference]]: Linux Plumbers Conference discussed live kernel patching approaches including CRIU+kexec, kGraft, kpatch, and ksplice. Each method trades off latency, reliability, and system downtime differently. The microconference aimed to find common ground among these competing solutions.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/607047/][Matthew Garrett calls for the private, secure desktop]]: Matthew Garrett's GUADEC 2014 keynote argued for GNOME to lead in security and privacy. He addressed hardware capabilities that could enable surveillance and the responsibilities desktop environments have in protecting users.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/607091/][Wayland in GNOME: two progress reports]]: GNOME's Wayland progress includes security improvements like separate surfaces for notification content and WebKitGTK+ now running on Wayland. These address longstanding concerns about Wayland's readiness for desktop use.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/606995/][Two paths to a better readdir()]]: Two approaches improve directory traversal: xgetdents() for batched reads and dirreadahead() for predictive caching. Both address inefficiencies in readdir() causing excessive syscalls and poor cache utilization.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608425/][Control groups, part 7: To unity and beyond]]: Final installment of Neil Brown's cgroups series evaluates the "unified hierarchy" introduced in Linux 3.16. Applies design pattern analysis (full exploitation, conflated designs, unfixable designs, high maintenance designs) to assess whether the unified hierarchy solves issues found in classic cgroups. Concludes with a score card rating unification benefits against complexity costs.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608501/][Merging GTK+ and Clutter]]: Emmanuele Bassi presented at GUADEC 2014 on merging GTK+ and Clutter scene-graph capabilities. The work aims to bring GPU-accelerated rendering to GTK+ through Clutter's actor-based scene graph, enabling smooth animations and transitions. Challenges include maintaining API stability while integrating the different event handling and drawing models.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608503/][BadUSB: Clever but not novel]]: Discusses the BadUSB vulnerability presented at Black Hat 2014, which allows USB firmware reprogramming to turn devices into attack tools. While clever, the attack relies on modifying USB device firmware—a known risk since USB design assumes devices are trustworthy. Argues the real issue is the lack of cryptographic authentication for USB devices.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608571/][Stultz: 2038 Kernel Summit Discussion Fodder]]: John Stultz's email to ksummit-discuss provides discussion fodder for the Year 2038 problem: 32-bit time_t overflow on January 19, 2038. Proposals include converting to 64-bit time_t (breaking user/kernel ABI), using y2038-safe internal representations, or runtime conversion. The challenge is maintaining compatibility while fixing a fundamental kernel data structure.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/608497/][Ftrace: The hidden light switch]]: Brendan Gregg explains using Ftrace (function tracer) to analyze Linux kernel behavior and performance. Covers function graph tracer, latency tracing, event tracing, and combining with perf for hierarchical analysis. Demonstrates how Ftrace reveals kernel execution paths that appear as a 'dark dungeon' without proper instrumentation.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/607117/][The RCU-tasks subsystem]]: RCU-tasks extends Linux RCU to track process (not CPU) references, using a holdout list of runnable tasks with scheduler hooks to clear flags on context switch. Unlike classic RCU, it adds a kernel thread polling every 100ms and handles tasks that exit while on the holdout list via lock-protected linked lists.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/606699/][Control groups, part 5: The cgroup hierarchy]]: Explores classic cgroup hierarchies where subsystems can attach to separate hierarchies, creating a "tyranny of choice" problem. Argues accounting subsystems (cpu, blkio, memory) benefit from separate hierarchies while non-accounting subsystems suit a single administrative hierarchy, though current implementations cannot properly charge resource usage across mismatched hierarchies.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/606697/][Kügler: Plasma's Road to Wayland]]: KDE Plasma's path to Wayland support required lifting the graphics stack to Qt5 and making X11 dependencies optional in Frameworks 5. Work remains on workspace components, the window manager, and compositor to handle non-X11 systems properly.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/606704/][Interview with Nathan Willis, GUADEC Keynote Speaker]]: Nathan Willis's GUADEC 2014 keynote argues the free software community should engage with automotive software, viewing the car as a first-rate environment for free software despite corporate projects having gaps that community involvement could fill.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/607164/][Akademy 2014 Keynotes: Sascha Meinrath and Cornelius Schumacher]]: KDE's Akademy 2014 opened with Sascha Meinrath speaking on societal navigation away from dangerous cliffs, while Cornelius Schumacher discussed KDE's identity and why it matters for the community.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/606572/][An Interview with Karen Sandler (Model View Culture)]]: Karen Sandler (Software Freedom Conservancy) discusses her experience as a "cyborg" with a pacemaker/defibrillator whose source code she cannot examine, arguing that free software is essential for safety-critical medical devices and other life-critical systems like voting machines and cars.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/606238/][Handling ARM architecture changes]]: ARM architecture evolution is breaking backward compatibility with older applications using deprecated instructions like SWP; the kernel community debates whether to emulate these instructions or require application rebuilds, eventually proposing a multi-stage deprecation timeline.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/606235/][Numbering the next major PHP release]]: The PHP community debated naming the next major release PHP 6 vs PHP 7 due to confusion with the abandoned 2005-2010 PHP 6 Unicode project; PHP 7 ultimately won the vote.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/606186/][Browser tracking through "canvas fingerprinting"]]: Canvas fingerprinting is a new browser tracking technique using HTML5 canvas rendering to produce unique browser fingerprints; AddThis was found using this method, though it can be blocked by Tor, NoScript, or Privacy Badger.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/606176/][Docker security with SELinux (Opensource.com)]]: Dan Walsh clarifies that Docker containers do not fully sandbox applications and require SELinux for proper security isolation between containers and the host system.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/605846/][Faults in Linux 2.6]]: A study of Linux 2.6 kernels (2003-2011) found that while the kernel more than doubled in size, fault rates per line of code decreased; drivers now have lower fault rates than the arch directory, with ppc and powerpc showing highest fault rates outside x86/i386, and moderately young files (8-26 months) having the highest fault rates at over 0.89%.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/606089/][A reworked BPF API]]: Version 2 of the BPF patch set introduces a single bpf() system call using file descriptors instead of global numeric IDs for programs and maps, with BPF_PROG_LOAD command and a 'fixup array' mechanism for converting file descriptors to internal map representations, targeting Linux 3.17.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/606141/][A system call for random numbers: getrandom()]]: A new getrandom() system call was proposed to provide a safe way for applications like LibreSSL to obtain random data, with blocking mode that waits for /dev/urandom initialization and GRND_NONBLOCK flag to return EAGAIN if the pool is not ready.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/606357/][Testing CentOS-6 to CentOS-7 upgrades]]: CentOS now offers beta testing RPMs for upgrading from CentOS 6 to CentOS 7, a process that typically requires complete re-install rather than in-place upgrade. Manuel Mausz's patches have significantly improved the Preupgrade Assistant, and the community is now invited to test these patched RPMs.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/606183/][Spencer: The Community Team]]: Ubuntu introduces David Planella as the new Community Team Manager, joining Michael Hall, Daniel Holbach, and Nicholas Skaggs on the community team. The role focuses on organizing and leading team members rather than "managing the community" directly.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/606044/][Xiki: blurring the distinction between an editor and a shell]]: Xiki is a project that creates a modern interactive command-line environment combining shell functionality with editor capabilities, blurring the line between editor and shell.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/606004/][Control groups, part 4: On accounting]]: This article explores Linux cgroup accounting subsystems including cpuacct (CPU time), memory/hugetlb (memory tracking via res_counter), cpu (scheduler runtime propagation), and blkio (I/O bandwidth throttling/scheduling); key design tradeoffs include hierarchical accounting where usage propagates to ancestors and batching strategies to minimize lock contention.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/606144/][Looking in on CentOS 7]]: CentOS 7 delivers 10 years of support with RHEL binary compatibility, featuring XFS as default filesystem, systemd as system/service manager, and Docker base images; specialized SIGs provide tailored builds for cloud computing, atomic/Docker hosts, VoIP, and simplified server configurations.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/605381/][On satellite and aerial imagery for free software]]: Examines free-software mapping tools like OsmAnd, which lacks satellite/aerial imagery support unlike Google Maps, exploring alternatives such as tile-based mapping projects and the challenges of obtaining high-resolution imagery for open-source navigation applications.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/605473/][Steam comes to Linux]]: Valve's Steam platform arrives on Linux, marking a significant shift in Linux gaming from primarily open-source titles to a commercial gaming ecosystem; covers the technical requirements, game compatibility layer, and implications for Linux as a desktop gaming platform.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/605409/][Genealogy research with Gramps]]: Gramps is presented as the primary open-source genealogy application, offering comprehensive family history tracking with features for recording ancestors, events, sources, and relationships; article explores its data model, import/export formats, and plugin ecosystem.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/605490/][The security of git.centos.org]]: Analyzes the security architecture of the RHEL source code repository now hosted at git.centos.org, examining access controls, commit verification mechanisms, and the trust model between Red Hat, CentOS developers, and community contributors.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/605445/][Google's "Project Zero"]]: Google's Project Zero initiative dedicated to finding and responsibly disclosing zero-day vulnerabilities across the internet, not just in Google products; the team uses novel detection techniques and aims to make the entire web safer by reducing the window of exploitability.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/604515/][Anatomy of a system call, part 2]]: Explores syscall mechanisms beyond basic x86_64, covering 32-bit x86 variants including SYSENTER instruction and INT 0x80; also covers x86_32 syscall invocation on x86_64, gettimeofday() vDSO mechanism, and ptrace() for syscall tracing.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/605039/][Control groups, part 3: First steps to control]]: Details the Linux 3.15 cgroups implementation covering seven subsystems (debug, net_cls, net_prio, devices, freezer, perf_event, cpuset) that independently manage process groupings with hierarchical challenges.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/605128/][Filesystem notification, part 2: A deeper investigation of inotify]]: Continues the inotify exploration, addressing dnotify limitations and providing detailed coverage of the Linux filesystem notification API for monitoring file system events.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/605059/][Boyer: At the playground]]: Fedora kernel team member Josh Boyer introduces the Fedora kernel-playground Copr, an unsupported repository providing kernels with new features for testing purposes.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/605607/][The glibc s390 ABI break]]: Analyzes a GNU C library conservatism issue where a mistake involving the s390 architecture cascaded into a significant ABI break, demonstrating how subtle changes can have far-reaching consequences in foundational libraries.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/605677/][State of Rust 0.11.0]]: Rust 0.11.0 release marks milestone with new GitHub organization, RFC process for language evolution, and initial plugin system for the compiler; covers the growing ecosystem, community governance changes, and future direction of the systems programming language.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/605064/][Sandboxed applications for GNOME]]: GNOME sandboxing initiative aims to improve desktop application security through containerization, restricting filesystem access, and limiting inter-process communication; article examines implementation challenges, usability tradeoffs, and implications for Linux desktop security.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/605678/][Mozilla: Improving JPEG Image Encoding]]: Mozilla's efforts to enhance JPEG compression quality in Firefox and other projects through improved encoding algorithms and optimization techniques; discusses the balance between image quality, file size, and encoding performance.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/602596/][A report from the first DockerCon]]: First DockerCon held in 2013 marked Docker's emergence as a containerization platform, showcasing how Linux containers evolved from a niche technology to a mainstream DevOps tool; covers the technical architecture of Docker's image layering and container runtime.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/602457/][Karen Sandler on what we mean by 'we']]: Karen Sandler (GNOME Foundation executive director) delivers keynote examining the philosophical meaning of "community" in free software, questioning whether corporate contributors diminish or strengthen the movement; explores the tension between individual rights and collective responsibility in open-source development.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/602650/][The volatile volatile ranges patch set]]: Linux kernel patch proposal for "volatile ranges" memory management feature to hint to the kernel which memory regions can be aggressively reclaimed under memory pressure; technical analysis of the interface design and its potential use cases for memory-efficient workloads.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/602479/][Teaching the scheduler about power management]]: Linux kernel scheduler enhancements for power-aware task placement, dynamically adjusting CPU frequency and core hotplug based on workload characteristics; examines the tradeoffs between performance and energy consumption in modern multicore systems.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/602521/][Android without the mothership]]: Explores the challenges and technical approaches for using Android devices without Google Play Services, covering alternative app stores, de-Googled ROMs like Replicant and CopperheadOS, and the implications for privacy, security updates, and ecosystem fragmentation.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/602428/][The history of Android (ars technica)]]: Ars Technica examines "cloud rot" in Android—the problem of apps becoming non-functional when cloud servers shut down, as demonstrated by defunct Google Maps and Android Market clients; raises concerns about digital preservation of cloud-dependent software compared to traditional locally-executable programs.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/602733/][Acilos, the private social networking "valet"]]: Acilos is an LGPL social media aggregation tool using Dojo, d3, and ElasticSearch that lets users manage accounts across Twitter, LinkedIn, Facebook, Instagram, and Google+ from a local machine; uses individual API keys per user rather than shared keys to improve privacy isolation.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/602569/][LibreOffice bug hunting event]]: The Document Foundation announces a three-day LibreOffice 4.3 bug hunting session (June 20-22, 2014) with QA volunteers helping test the release candidate; enterprise and individual users without prior QA experience can participate via mailing list and IRC.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/605382/][Justin Miller on how Mapbox runs like an open source project]]: Mapbox runs its business like an open source project with a flat, open organizational structure where developers join projects based on interest or demonstrate code potential to gain traction; nearly all their code is open source and reusable by others.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/602693/][Running Firefox OS apps on Android]]: Firefox for Android (v29) can now serve as a web runtime for HTML5 web apps packaged for Firefox OS, reusing Android's kernel and HAL while running Mozilla's Gecko engine; allows Firefox OS apps from the Firefox Marketplace to run on Android devices.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/599755/][BPF: the universal in-kernel virtual machine]]: BPF (Berkeley Packet Filter) evolved from a simple packet-filtering language for tcpdump into a powerful in-kernel virtual machine; the 3.15 kernel split it into "classic" and "internal" variants, the latter expanding registers from two to ten with 64-bit support and controlled kernel function calls, remaining hidden from user space while enabling better code generation and broader subsystem integration.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/601840/][The unified control group hierarchy in 3.16]]: The 3.16 kernel introduces a unified control group hierarchy replacing the flexible but costly multiple-hierarchy approach; the unified design eliminates expensive per-process controller tracking and enables better cooperation between controllers (e.g., memory and block I/O) at the cost of some organizational flexibility.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/601910/][The Tizen SDK for wearable devices]]: The Tizen SDK for Wearable supports developing apps for Samsung's Gear 2 smartwatch and similar devices with an Eclipse-based IDE and APIs from the Tizen Wearable device profile; development focuses primarily on tethering to smartphones and tablets, with standalone app support also available, using Samsung-proprietary components on top of the open-source SDK.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/601912/][What's in a (CentOS) version number?]]: A proposal to change CentOS version numbering sparked debate about community trust following Red Hat's acquisition of the project; traditionally mirroring RHEL version numbers, any deviation raised concerns about the project's independence and long-term sustainability, highlighting tension between Red Hat's support (full-time developers, infrastructure) and fears of losing a freely-distributable RHEL equivalent.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/602060/][Tizen's new access-control broker "Cynara"]]: Tizen 3.0 introduces Cynara, a privilege-checking service replacing the unwieldy Smack-based security policy from Tizen 2.x, which created per-application Smack domains resulting in a massive, hard-to-maintain policy database; the new design uses a simplified three-domain policy model with all installed apps at one basic privilege level.


- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/602077/][Krita Kicks Off 2.9 Development Effort with a Kickstarter Campaign]]: Krita's crowdfunding campaign to fund a full-time developer for the 2.9 release, with a stretch goal to fund an additional full-time developer.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/602562/][Android Root Access Vulnerability Affecting Most Devices (Threatpost)]]: The TowelRoot exploit (CVE-2014-3153) gave administrative access to Android devices via a Linux kernel privilege escalation bug, potentially bypassing enterprise security solutions including Samsung Knox.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/605843/][The state of accessibility in Linux and open source software (Opensource.com)]]: Interview with Spencer Hunley on the lack of easily discoverable built-in accessibility support in most Linux distributions, emphasizing the need for independence-focused features for users with disabilities.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/606377/][s390 glibc jmp_buf change to be reverted]]: Ulrich Weigand reverted the glibc 2.19 jmp_buf size change on s390/s390x after discovering it broke real-world binaries, accepting that new registers must remain call-clobbered in the ABI to avoid breaking older binaries.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612888/][Best practices for the new era of open source (opensource.com)]]: Using Ansible as a model project, the article argues that rapid first-time user experience and comprehensibility are critical drivers of open source software adoption, and that too many projects fail by not investing in this principle.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612961/][Bug-Squashing-Party in Munich, Germany]]: A Debian Bug-Squashing-Party in Munich (Nov 21-23, 2014) hosted by the LiMux Linux Client Team, co-located with Kubuntu and KDE PIM sessions, with capacity for up to 40 attendees and open to non-DDs.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/612975/][Gentoo discusses a Git migration]]: Michał Górny proposed a master plan for migrating Gentoo's CVS repositories to Git in under two weeks, envisioning three separate repos—developer push, user sync (read-only with extras), and rsync—sparking discussion on the feasibility of the long-anticipated transition.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/613006/][Who wrote 3.15 through 3.17]]: Jonathan Corbet's kernel statistics covering three release cycles (38,679 changesets from 2,546 developers, +593K lines) show per-release developer counts rising above 1,400 for the first time, and that 3.17 actually shrank due to staging-tree cleanup—only the second such occurrence in kernel history.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/613034/][New From Rocky Nook -- Advanced Software Testing—Vol. 2, 2nd Edition]]: Rocky Nook announced the second edition of Rex Black's ISTQB Advanced Test Manager guide, updated to the 2012 syllabus with exercises on test estimation, planning, monitoring, and team leadership, plus sample exam questions.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/613041/][Mailpile's first beta release]]: The Mailpile project released v0.4.0 beta, delivering a self-hosted, privacy-focused webmail experience with guided setup (requires OpenSSL, GnuPG, Python 2) and a polished HTML/JS UI, using GnuPG to encrypt both stored and sent email in a GMail-like interface free of ads and tracking.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/616238/][The POODLE vulnerability]]: Google disclosed POODLE, an SSL 3.0 vulnerability that lets MITM attackers force protocol downgrade and recover plaintext from CBC-mode ciphertext. Mitigation requires disabling SSL 3.0 or adopting TLS_FALLBACK_SCSV to prevent downgrade attacks, with OpenSSL releasing a corresponding advisory.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/616241/][A damp discussion of network queuing]]: At Linux Plumbers Conference 2014, Stephen Hemminger demonstrated bufferbloat using water bottles and discussed solutions including CoDel (Controlled Delay) and fq_codel, which drops packets queued too long (5ms default). He recommended `sysctl -w net.core.default_qdisc=fq_codel` to apply the fair-queuing CoDel discipline to all connections.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/616317/][A new OpenSSL vulnerability]]: A related POODLE attack on OpenSSL exploits browser-initiated TLS-to-SSL 3.0 downgrades to recover plaintext via MITM. Mozilla disabled SSL 3.0 by default in Firefox 34; two additional memory-leak DoS vulnerabilities and a build-option bug (no-ssl3 not fully effective) were also disclosed.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/616512/][GCC Undefined Behavior Sanitizer - ubsan (RH Developer Blog)]]: Red Hat Developer Blog covers ubsan, a runtime undefined behavior checker added in GCC 4.9 that detects signed integer overflow and other UB in real programs. It requires `-fsanitize=undefined` in both CFLAGS and LDFLAGS, with additional bounds/alignment checks available in GCC 5.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/617452/][Etcd and fleet]]: CoreOS's Brandon Philips presented etcd, a distributed key-value store using the Raft consensus algorithm for cluster configuration with sequential consistency and compare-and-swap operations. Fleet extends this as a systemd-based cluster scheduler that distributes tasks across machines, rescheduling on failure.



- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/618197/][A Wayland full-screen shell]]: Jason Ekstrand proposed compositor nesting to handle screen sharing and recording in Wayland, avoiding per-compositor protocol implementations. Instead of embedding screen-capture and input-recording protocols into every compositor, a dedicated nested compositor process handles them, leveraging the existing Wayland architecture where compositors talk to both hardware and clients.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/619068/][Kdbus meets linux-kernel]]: Greg Kroah-Hartman posted kdbus, an in-kernel D-Bus implementation, for linux-kernel review after two years of development. Kdbus creates /dev/kdbus device nodes for interprocess messaging with per-container domain hierarchies, zero-copy message transfer for large payloads, and built-in policy enforcement for secure IPC. The discussion highlighted tensions between kernel-space complexity and userspace alternatives.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/619150/][Videos from the GNU Tools Cauldron]]: A full set of videos from the 2014 GNU Tools Cauldron conference covering GCC, glibc, GDB, and other low-level toolchain topics is now available online.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/619167/][The Book of CSS3, 2nd Edition]]: Peter Gasston's second edition (No Starch Press) covers updated CSS3 syntax including gradients, grids, flexible box layout, filters, and blend modes with real-world examples for experienced web developers.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/619169/][How Linux Works, 2nd Edition]]: Brian Ward's completely revised second edition (No Starch Press) provides in-depth coverage of Linux boot, kernel internals, system calls, I/O, filesystems, networking, and shell scripting, combining theory with real-world administration examples.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/619217/][Yocto training materials published]]: Free Electrons released Yocto Project and OpenEmbedded training materials under the Creative Commons Attribution Share-Alike license, available in PDF format with accompanying LaTeX source code.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/619236/][The Dronecode collaborative project]]: The Linux Foundation launched Dronecode, an open-source UAV software collaboration at LinuxCon Europe, with founding members including 3D Robotics, Intel, and Qualcomm. The project consolidates two autopilot projects—APM for higher-end fixed-wing systems and PX4 for DIY-class devices—along with MissionPlanner, DroidPlanner, and MavLink, driven by FAA regulatory concerns and a projected $91B commercial drone market.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/619355/][Recent read-mostly research]]: Paul McKenney surveys recent academic RCU research covering validation of memory barriers and data-race detectors, RCU-protected red-black trees with transactional memory, resizable hash tables, Bonsai balanced binary trees for reducing mmap_sem contention, Masstree combining B+ trees and tries, linearizable RCU search trees, and passive reader-writer locks using IPI-based read-to-write transitions.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/619381/][Kügler: Diving into Plasma's 2015]]: Sebastian Kügler outlines KDE Plasma 5's roadmap including high-DPI and Wayland support, with distributions like Kubuntu 15.04 planning to ship Plasma 5 as the default desktop environment, potentially reaching 10 million users by year's end.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/619505/][Peck: New GIMP Save/Export plug-in: Saver]]: Akkana Peck announces the Saver GIMP plugin to replace the controversial Save/Export separation introduced in GIMP 2.8. Saver contextually overwrites JPEGs on Ctrl-S for quick edits, saves .xcf.gz for multi-layer projects, and opens a Save As dialog on Ctrl-Shift-S, with the option to save both XCF and JPG copies simultaneously.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/619738/][Transparent huge page reference counting]]: Kirill A. Shutemov's patch set aims to reduce limitations in the kernel's transparent huge pages (THP) feature by modifying complex memory-management code. THP automatically substitutes huge pages into a process's address space, but currently avoids page-cache huge pages and uses split_huge_page() to break down huge pages when code cannot handle them, incurring performance penalties that the patch set seeks to minimize.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/619751/][Meeks: OpenGL rendering for LibreOffice 4.4]]: Michael Meeks describes moving image interpolation to the GPU via OpenGL rendering to resolve the tradeoff between rendering quality and performance in LibreOffice. Offloading this work to the GPU enables high-quality scaled images without sacrificing speed, making rendering both pretty and fast.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/619752/][Baker: Mozilla and the Future of the Open Internet]]: Mitchell Baker reflects on Firefox's 10th anniversary, asserting that Firefox succeeded in the desktop era by fundamentally changing the web landscape. She warns that the Web still faces real threats today, emphasizing that continued vigilance is needed to protect the open Internet.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/619795/][Meteor: A framework for web applications]]: Meteor is an open-source full-stack platform for building web and mobile apps in pure JavaScript with live-updating interfaces. Its real-time system (Blaze and DDP) instantly propagates database changes to clients, distinguishing it from server-centric frameworks like Ruby on Rails. The 1.0 release was celebrated globally on November 6, 2014.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/620050/][Microsoft open-sources the .NET core]]: Microsoft released the .NET core code under an MIT open-source license on GitHub, noting that the GitHub platform attracts far more contributions than CodePlex. The Mono community had been forced to re-implement .NET from scratch because no open source implementation was available, and this release unifies the previously separate Windows and Mono code bases.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/622681/][A preview of darktable 1.6]]: Darktable 1.6 RC adds slideshow mode, a defringe operation for correcting longitudinal chromatic aberration in raw photos, and enhanced basecurve tool with camera-maker preset curves. The release also improves image import control and upgrades existing tools, with the final version expected before the end of 2014, roughly a year after the 1.4 stable release.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/622988/][Touring the hidden corners of LWN]]: LWN introduces new subscriber features including the ability to filter out non-subscriber comments and a responsive design preview that scales across devices from phones to desktops. The article also highlights existing but lesser-known features: commenter filtering (available since 2010), ad removal for professional-tier subscribers, parent-comment context in unread-comments view, and daily/headline mailing lists for tracking LWN content.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/625070/][Kocialkowski: A hacker's journey: freeing a phone from the ground up, first part]]: Paul Kocialkowski recounts porting Replicant (fully free Android distribution) to the LG Optimus Black (P970), picking up after a community member stalled on driver and hardware-compatibility issues. The journey involves reverse-engineering proprietary components and replacing them with free-software alternatives to create a phone usable without any proprietary code.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/625313/][Eloquent JavaScript, 2nd Edition - New from No Starch Press]]: Marijn Haverbeke's thoroughly revised second edition (No Starch Press, Dec 2014) more than doubles the original's length with new chapters on Node.js, graphics programming, and code performance. Readers build projects including an artificial life simulation, a simple programming language, and a paint program while learning OOP, functional programming, DOM scripting, and server-side JavaScript.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/625570/][A Ruby Programming Book for Kids]]: "Ruby Wizardry" by Eric Weinstein (No Starch Press, Dec 2014) teaches children programming through a story-based adventure following characters Ruben and Scarlet, covering variables, loops, conditionals, classes, methods, and interactive storytelling in Ruby. The book targets the growing learn-to-code movement, addressing the gap where fewer than 10% of US schools offer programming classes despite over 120,000 unfilled computing jobs annually.
